HEADLINE: TELEVISION/RADIO;
Getting Down to What Makes America High

BYLINE: By ANDY MEISLER; Andy Meisler's most recent article for Arts & Leisure was about the animated series "Dilbert."

DATELINE: UNIVERSAL CITY, Calif.

 BODY:
SEVERAL years ago, the Oscar-winning director Robert Zemeckis was making a movie on location when he discovered that one of his principal cast members had a serious drinking problem.
The actor failed to show up for a night scene he was to appear in. After a costly delay, it was discovered that a local bartender, having watched the actor drink himself into insensibility, had driven him back to his motel. 
 As Mr. Zemeckis remembered it during an interview in his office on the Universal Studios backlot, his immediate reaction was swift and terrible: at 2 A.M. he phoned the local doctor who was on call for the film company, summoned him to the set and proceeded to berate him, loudly blaming him and him alone for the actor's inebriated state.
"Irrational? Of course," Mr. Zemeckis said. "I would hope that I wouldn't react the same way again. But it was simply the unbelievable frustration of not being in control of this problem."
He shook his head ruefully. His story, he admitted, is a "perfect parable" for society's chronically wrongheaded approach to the complicated problem of addiction.
"One thing that quickly becomes apparent," he said, "is that there is no solution to the problem. You can't control what is basically a personal journey. You can't legislate sobriety."
Not that American legislators haven't tried. In fact, Mr. Zemeckis -- a 48-year-old Hollywood filmmaker not formerly even vaguely associated with the low-profile world of nonfiction television -- has made a two-hour documentary on this very subject.
Titled "The Pursuit of Happiness: Smoking, Drinking and Drugging in the 20th Century," the film is to have its premiere on the Showtime cable network on Sept. 13 as part of "In the 20th Century," a millennium-related series using well-known directors to take on major subjects of their choosing. It is a rare full-length examination of a topic that lends itself easily to rousing slogans and pithy sound bites.
Indeed, it is with the help of dozens of these fleeting but oh-so-emphatic references to drugs, alcohol, tobacco and addiction that Mr. Zemeckis makes his main points. He culled them from sources as varied as Presidential press conferences, the utterances of Presidential "drug czars," newsreels, beer commercials, old magazine ads, old sitcoms like "Leave It to Beaver," standup comedy routines, exploitation films like "Reefer Madness" and Hollywood blockbusters (including his own).
"We came up with hundreds of hours of wonderful material," said Mr. Zemeckis. One can see, for example, Desi Arnaz hawking Camels, Newt Gingrich advocating the death penalty for drug dealing, Jack Webb pontificating on the horrors of LSD, a gaggle of tobacco executives swearing that cigarettes aren't addictive and a psychedelically dressed Sonny Bono urging his fans "not to become potheads."
Interspersed with these half-forgotten audiovisual artifacts are on-camera interviews with numerous historians, former addicts, addiction counselors and other experts, including a spokesman for Alcoholics Anonymous, an advocate of medical marijuana use, a libertarian proponent of drug decriminalization, the author of "Denial Is Not a River in Egypt" and the radio personality Dr. Demento, a connoisseur of drug-soaked pop music from Cab Calloway to Kurt Cobain.
"It was amazing what we discovered," said Mr. Zemeckis. "For instance: when I started this project, I thought that the so-called drug crisis was something that was happening right now; our generation's problem, as bad as it's ever been. But it turns out that the crisis has been going on forever -- and actually, the worst period in our country's history was the 1830's, when the entire country was on a bender and everybody was walking around drunk. Actually, the country has been sobering up pretty interestingly since then."
Among the other arguments made in "The Pursuit of Happiness": that American society has habitually criminalized the substances used primarily by minorities (i.e., opium for Asian immigrants and marijuana and cocaine for African-Americans) while legalizing those used by white adults (i.e., beer, alcohol and tobacco). That the advent of Prohibition in the 1920's, far from springing full-blown from a groundswell of public indignation, was in large part an economic imperative pushed by industrialists like Henry Ford who wanted reliable, sober workers for their new assembly lines. That antidrug public service announcements and school programs, though well-meaning and organized with creativity and passion, have had little proven effect on drug use.
Also: that the search for pleasure, chemical-based or otherwise, is a not "problem" that can be addressed as if it somehow stands apart from our very identities as Americans and human beings. One of the most striking moments in the documentary is at the beginning when an actor recites the "pursuit of happiness" clause in the Declaration of Independence against a background of images ranging from public inebriation to a smiling little girl twirling herself dizzy on a tree swing.
"You know," intones one of the experts, "the impulse to alter what we ordinarily call ordinary waking consciousness is inborn."
Perhaps the most provocative aspect of "Smoking, Drinking and Drugging" is that its approach is accepted by the very experts and authority figures usually arrayed on the other side of the ledger.
"Considering that it wasn't intended to be an antidrug piece, but a comprehensive and sophisticated treatment of a very complex issue, I thought it was great," said Ginna Marston, one of Mr. Zemeckis's interviewees and a spokeswoman for Parternship for a Drug-Free America, the advertising-industry coalition that produces the well-known antidrug public service announcements ("This is your brain on drugs").
"When we signed on to Bob doing this," Mark Zakarin, Showtime's executive vice president for original programming, said carefully, "we realized that he was going to bring a point of view to it. All we asked was there be an intelligent framework to his subjectivity, and inside that framework enough objective reality that we can all agree on."
BEFORE his current project, the sum of Mr. Zemeckis's experience with the documentary form was in the early 1970's, during a short stint as an assistant news editor at a Chicago television station. Mr. Zakarin maintains that inexperience was never a problem. Mr. Zemeckis didn't see it as an obstacle, either.
"I'm addicted to documentaries," he said. "That's all I watch on television. It started with "The American Experience," on PBS, and now I watch the History Channel, the Learning Channel -- everything."
Mr. Zemeckis was born and reared in the Chicago area. He says he was a heavy drinker in college -- a habit, he adds, that disappeared once he transferred to film school at the University of Southern California. After making several unsuccessful feature films in the 1970's and early 1980's, he had a run of hits, beginning with "Romancing the Stone" in 1984. Soon after came "Back to the Future" (1985), "Who Framed Roger Rabbit?" (1988) and "Forrest Gump" (1994). For the latter, he won the Academy Award for best director.
After finishing "Contact" with Jodie Foster in 1997, he took some time off and began to think about doing a documentary. A magazine article caught his eye, about the Gold Cure, a turn-of-the-century treatment for alcoholism, quite popular at the time, that in hindsight was totally ineffective.
"I said to myself, 'You know, in all of the documentaries I've seen, I've never heard anybody talk about the history of addiction," he said.
He then got in touch with the producers of the PBS history series "The American Experience," who told him that they had already broadcast an episode about the temperance movement. They referred him to Showtime.
That network signed him up for "In the 20th Century" (the other directors participating are Garry Marshall, Barry Levinson, Robert Townshend, Norman Jewison and Gregory Nava); approved a $500,000 budget; referred him to Susan Emerling, a writer-researcher who has worked with the noted documentarian Barbara Kopple, and gave him the go-ahead.
Mr. Zemeckis, who conducted each of the original interviews in the documentary, said he had particularly enjoyed working in the editing room, finding the story he wanted to tell amid miles of film and tape.
Much about the subject was depressing and confusing, he said, but he added that he had no hesitation screening his finished product for his 13-year-old son.
"Yeah, sure, I worry about him getting involved with those things," said Mr. Zemeckis. "And, sure, I wonder what I would say to him when the time comes.
"In fact, that's the last question I asked of everyone I interviewed. And almost all of them told me the same thing: ultimately, all you can do is love them and tell them the truth."

SECTION: Section 11; Page 9; Column 1; Real Estate Desk 

LENGTH: 1510 words

HEADLINE: In the Region/Long Island;
The Troubling Effect of a New Nassau Transfer Tax

BYLINE: By DIANA SHAMAN 

 BODY:
WARREN REDEKER feels it is his hard luck that the three-bedroom ranch in Mineola that he has owned as an investment for the last 10 years and recently placed on the market for $240,000 is in Nassau County and not in Suffolk.
For come closing time, assuming that he receives his asking price, Mr. Redeker will have to write a $2,400 check to the Nassau County Clerk to comply with a 1 percent real estate transfer tax that the county recently imposed on sellers. The new tax, which cannot be listed as a deduction on Federal tax returns, is the highest in the state, equaled only by New York City's. 
 The new legislation affects any real estate sale without a binding contract and a down payment deposited in a bank before Aug. 15. The tax, which is levied on the entire selling price, is being added to the existing New York State transfer tax, which amounts to 0.4 percent of the selling price.
"I am pretty angry because the only reason this tax has been imposed is to make up for the shortfalls in the Nassau County budget and that, basically, was due to incompetence," said Mr. Redeker, who works in Manhattan as an insurance agent.
Dubbed the Gulotta Tax after Thomas S. Gulotta, Nassau's Republican County Executive, the new tax is sending storm signals through the local real estate industry, with predictions that already expensive housing in Nassau will get even more expensive if owners pass along the new tax to buyers.
"In a seller's market, the world is your oyster and if you have any savvy you will either increase the price or say to the buyer, you can buy at this price but you have to absorb the tax," said Howard W. Goldson, a Commack lawyer who is counsel to the Long Island Board of Realtors.
But sellers say raising prices is not so easily done. "My house is only worth what someone is going to pay for it," Mr. Redeker said. "The way I see it, it's money out of my pocket."
The tax is particularly onerous because it "singles out a small segment of the population and penalizes them, and that isn't right," said State Assemblyman David S. Sidikman of Old Bethpage. Mr. Sidikman and a majority of his fellow Democrats in the State Legislature opposed the tax, which needed state consent, but the measure was approved Aug. 3 along with other state budget bills. The 18-member County Legislature, with all four Democrats opposed, imposed the tax Aug. 9.
The transfer tax is expected to generate $50 million to $60 million annually for the county. David A. Vieser, a spokesman for Mr. Gulotta, said it was the first of many measures the county would be taking to address the $192.3 million deficit it had incurred as of the end of 1998. The transfer tax was implemented first, he said, because it had to be acted upon before the State Legislature went into recess.
Many homeowners placing their houses on the market are older people "for whom this is their retirement fund," Mr. Sidikman said. "They have raised their children, paid their taxes, been good citizens and now that they are about to leave, the Republican Party of Nassau County says, 'Thanks a lot, give us 1 percent as a goodbye present.' "
The tax must be paid on any transfer of property, both residential and commercial, including co-ops and vacant land. Under a sunset provision, it is to expire Jan. 31, 2001.
ONE option sellers may turn to is to pull their houses off the market for the next 18 months "causing a shortage and an increase in prices because the market is already tight," said Michael McHugh, the president of Continental Capital, a mortgage banking firm in Huntington and a member of the executive board of the Empire State Mortgage Bankers Association.
Desmond Ryan, the executive director of the Association for a Better Long Island, a real estate lobbying group in Hauppauge, said many commercial and industrial developers had been telling him that they were putting their sales on hold.
"The impact of this will be behavior modification," he said, "Cash-rich developers will say, 'We'll wait' " until the tax expires. Or, if they need cash, he added, "they'll refinance the building."
Adding a 1 percent mortgage tax to the .75 percent state mortgage tax currently paid by buyers taking out mortgages had also been under discussion, but the transfer tax was adopted instead.
"They dropped the mortgage tax because they were afraid people wouldn't move to Nassau," said Barbara Frechter, a Forest Hills, Queens, real estate broker who is the president of the Long Island Board of Realtors. "The transfer tax is evil, but it's the lesser of two evils because sellers are receiving money and it doesn't hurt as much to take the money from the proceeds."
On the other hand, Ms. Frechter noted, some sellers -- those facing foreclosure, for example -- walk away with very little, if any, cash, as do those who have to pay off high mortgages.
But Ms. Frechter predicted that in many cases buyers will wind up paying the tax, especially in today's strong real estate market.
"Sellers always want to know what they will net on a house after they pay off the mortgage and all their expenses," she said. "I believe they will add the tax on to the price of the house and that is going to hurt a lot of young people buying homes because they already have a difficult time even with two people working."
When builders sell houses, 9 out of 10 times they transfer all tax obligations over to buyers at the closing, said Robert A. Weiboldt, the executive director of the Long Island Builders Institute in Hauppauge. "So this will mean a 1 percent increase on the price of new houses in Nassau County," he said. "And all the buyer is getting for that 1 percent is the privilege of moving into a high tax area like Nassau."
For the purchaser of a new $250,000 house, for example, combined state and county transfer taxes will now be $3,500 instead of $1,000 paid previously.
"This will really hurt the real estate market because it will price some people out of the market," said James J. Lynch, a Williston Park real estate lawyer. He reasoned that the transfer tax was adopted because "it is an immediate gratification tax." County property tax increases could not have been collected until 2001, he said, "and the county couldn't wait."
Randy L. Kaplan, manager of media and government relations at the Board of Realtors, called the new law a "Band-Aid" that did not address such ills as inequitable property-tax assessments, which are costing the county millions annually in tax refunds.
"All around the country, they are trying to generate programs that will foster homeownership," he said. "Why does Nassau County have to turn around and do something detrimental to the American dream?"
W E also did not feel it appropriate for the county to add new taxes without putting out a complete comprehensive plan that looks at additional alternatives for paying off short- and long-term debt," said Mitchell Pally, vice president of legislative and economic affairs at the Long Island Association, a Melville-based business group.
Robert Siegler, a retired executive of a communications company, with a two-family investment house to sell in Williston Park said he never realized a new tax was even being discussed until his broker, Evelyn Atanas of Williston Park, informed him when he put his house on the market.
"It was a very sneaky way of putting across a tax, which most certainly hurts everybody," he said.
He and his wife, Marie, are asking $340,000 for the house.
"I don't believe I can get a higher price, but I may not accept an offer that I might have accepted before the tax went into effect, so this will probably drive up the cost for buyers," Mr. Siegler said.
They had been looking in Nassau for a condominium to replace their Melville residence, which they are also selling, he noted. But the tax, which he said he doubts will ever be lifted, is giving them second thoughts. "Now we're looking in the Smithtown direction," he said.
Still, few real estate professionals worry that the new transfer tax will sway most sellers or buyers.
"If they are serious sellers and have to move, they are going to sell and not dramatically change their plans," said Audrey Livingstone, owner of the Century 21 Annettes real estate agency in Uniondale.
"Nassau is very desirable place and we have a lot of people who want to live there," said Ms. Frechter, the Board of Realtors president. "But it's the lower-priced houses that worry me because of the cost to young couples."
On the East End of Suffolk a 2 percent transfer tax earmarked for the purchase of open space was approved by voters last November and took effect April 1. But there, it is the buyer who pays the tax and exemptions, depending on which town, ranging from the first $150,000 to the first $250,000 of the price of a house, were implemented to protect moderate-income buyers.
"People are not smiling when they pay the tax," said Michael DeSario, a partner of the Cook Pony Farm brokerage in East Hampton. "But business is booming."

HEADLINE: In the Region/Connecticut;
Lumber Shortage Raises Cost of Building a House

BYLINE: By ELEANOR CHARLES 

 BODY:
ALL set to sign that contract to build your new home? Don't be surprised if your builder has inserted a clause saying that if the price of lumber goes up during construction, so will the price of your house.
Shortages of lumber in the Northeast, caused in part by a home-building boom that put demand far ahead of supply, resulted in a 30 percent rise in prices from January to July of this year. 
 For an average 2,000-square-foot home the increase in the cost of lumber is about $4,000, according to Joanne Carroll, a director of the Connecticut Home Builders' Association and president of JMC Resources, a housing research firm. The increase for a trophy house in a swank Connecticut suburb can spiral up by $10,000 or more.
"A 6,000-square-foot house can take six months to build and make the buyer a victim of the market," said Norton C. Wheeler, lumber buyer for United Builders' Supply in Waterford and Niantic, Conn., and in Richmond and Westerly, R.I. "But at the lower end, even though a small house goes up faster, a sharp rise in lumber costs can knock a first-time buyer out of the box."
In recent years shortages have been exacerbated by cutbacks in Canadian lumber, the primary source for homes in the Northeastern United States, that began in 1996 when a quota on importing Canadian lumber was imposed to protect United States prices. Shipments fell from 16.2 billion to 14.7 billion board feet annually, said Nsimba Kinuani, chief economist for the Quebec Lumber Manufacturers' Association.
Other cutbacks resulted from a series of Federal logging restrictions that began in 1990, when 40 percent of American lumber came from Federal forests. Today the figure is down to 10.7 percent, said Butch Bernhardt, spokesman for the Western Wood Products Association in Portland, Ore. "Production in 1987 was 23.9 billion board feet," he said. "Last year it was 16.8 billion board feet."
Increasing volatility in the price of lumber has led developers and builders to seek more stability in overall expenses by focusing on eliminating waste and reducing labor costs. Labor has been a problem anyway. What had been a large pool of skilled construction workers has shrunk to a puddle. Builders believe that the men are finding less stressful jobs in a robust economy that in Connecticut has resulted in a 3 to 4 percent unemployment rate.
An enormous time and labor saver is the truss -- for floors and roofs. Manufactured of middle- and lower-grade woods under extreme pressure and delivered ready to install, it uses leftover wood and is stronger than conventional framing lumber. Trusses do not pop nails and they can be fabricated to lengths of as much as 80 feet. Floor trusses render floors virtually squeak proof and they have no need for supporting cellar beams and columns.
Another improvement in construction efficiency is panelization -- in which complete sections of framing that can extend to a width of 22 feet and a height of 12 feet are delivered ready to install, with exterior sheathing attached if desired. They require one or two unskilled laborers to set in place, rather than half a dozen experienced carpenters to build them from scratch.
While these elements are more expensive than traditional lumber, the extra cost is compensated for by savings in labor, construction time and even paperwork. A few bills for completed trusses and panels cover what hundreds of invoices for dozens of types and sizes of lumber, nails and other hardware cover in conventional construction.
Carl Kuehner, president of Building & Land Technologies in Wilton, builders of subdivisions and custom homes, has been using trusses for a few years, but just started panelization. He enumerated some of the advantages.
"Panels are numbered in sequence so you can't make a mistake," he said. "You don't have deliveries of 2- by-4's that are wrong and have to be returned, or wood that's cut wrong by a carpenter on site. Every nail is in place so there are no worries about lazy laborers skimping on nails. There is much less time for the building to be exposed to the elements, and you don't have to pick up leftover lumber."
A COMPANY in Shelton called B.T. Building Systems produces trusses, panels and a complete line of components for houses. The year-old firm describes itself as the only fully automated building component manufacturer on the East Coast. Named for its owners, Arnold Van H. Bernhard and Harold C. Thomas 3d, it is also a home builder. In executing what it calls its Fast Frame system, it can produce the framing, roof and floor trusses for a home or apartment complex to precise measurements in record time. The blueprints are entered into a computer and the building is produced by the stroke of a button that sets computerized saws and assembly machines in motion.
"The factory can do from four to six 3,000-square-foot homes in a single eight-hour shift," said Duffy Sasser, plant manager. "Where a crew of five carpenters might take 20 minutes to make and stand a wall, we can run 20 walls in that time. Framing is the biggest time consumer," he said. The words wall or panel in this context mean a "wall" of pre-assembled framing for a house.
For some developers and builders, taking the sting out of skyrocketing lumber prices can be accomplished by playing the commodities market.
William A. Ferrigno, owner-president of Sunlight Construction in Avon, builders of homes priced from $250,000 to over $1 million, invests in lumber futures. "We bought a 5-month lumber futures contract in February and made about $20,000 when prices peaked in July, but we spent $16,000 on product for three houses," he said, resulting in a modest $4,000 profit minus a $100 brokerage fee. Better than nothing, or a total loss in a down market, he said. While there is risk involved, most big builders have their sources within the industry for predicting major price swings.
Mr. Kuehner is also "hedging the market," he said, "either by buying lumber futures or getting the vendors to lock in the price for a certain period -- maybe six months. We don't sell any houses beyond that. In five months we reset the price based on the market." He noted that small builders don't have those options.
According to John W. Hall, head of J. M. Heinike, lumber wholesalers in Saratoga Springs, big-box stores like Home Depot and Lowe's are major lumber shortage generators. "If I'm a big consumer," he said, "I want to insure my supply, so I go to a big sawmill and get a contract based on a published lumber price every Friday. That sawmill may end up with 70 percent of the output tied to that contract, so only 30 percent is traded freely on the market, driving up the price."
Since late July, he said, people stopped buying wood, adding that it was "an emotional thing -- too expensive." Distributors lowered prices in August to entice buyers, Mr. Hall said, then raised them a few days later but not to their former high levels.
Mr. Wheeler believes that in the next few weeks distributors will begin to ship lumber ordered at the lower price. And what that means, he said, "is that prices will trickle down to the builder and home buyer in September."
A strong competitor for wood trusses, as well as an alternative to conventional framing lumber, is steel. Popular in California, Florida and Hawaii, it has not caught on in the Northeast. "There have been a few custom homes in Connecticut and Long Island, and it is being used in the Nehemiah projects -- subsidized low cost housing in Brooklyn and the Bronx," said John Pugliese, technical engineer at Super Stud Building Products in New York City.
Its advantages are its light weight and strength that is as great or greater than lumber. Greater truss spans can be produced simply by changing the gauge. "Steel doesn't burn, so insurance costs are lower, and steel prices are a lot more stable than lumber," he said.
But builders resist having to train crews in steel, teaching them to use screws instead of nails, eliminate saw cutting, and builders face higher plumbing and electrical costs because those trades won't work on steel-framed jobs at regular prices.
Steel's acceptance in other regions, said Lisa Stevens, marketing director for the North American Steel Framing Alliance in Washington, is based on practical considerations. It is impervious to Hawaii's hordes of termites, resistant to earthquake damage in California and it withstands Florida hurricanes.
Hurricanes also have a more direct effect on the lumber market. Lumberyards and construction companies in locations that are susceptible to hurricanes stock up on half-inch plywood, which is American made and now sells wholesale for about $2.88 for a 4- by 8-foot sheet. "Hurricane season shoots up the price of plywood," said Mr. Wheeler.

HEADLINE: In the Region /New Jersey;
Long Branch Hopes to Start Shore Redevelopment

BYLINE: By RACHELLE GARBARINE 

 BODY:
IN 1997, Long Branch put in place an innovative redevelopment plan to transform 150 acres of its faded Atlantic Ocean coastline into a new village.
The plan -- which not only sets guidelines for developers but incorporates the state's coastal development regulations into local zoning -- attracted interest from developers and last year the city started negotiating with three to rebuild its shoreline. But none moved forward, leaving the redevelopment district mostly desolate and the plan untested. The plan -- the result of years of discussions among city officials, residents and business groups and known as Long Branch Tomorrow -- was developed by the Thompson Design Group of Boston, which designed Pier 17 and the Fulton Market Building at South Street Seaport in Manhattan. 
 "I would not say we are frustrated," said Howard H. Woolley Jr., the business administrator for this seven-square-mile city with 29,000 residents. "But we are anxious to finalize a deal and get a shovel in the ground."
Local officials are hopeful that will soon happen and start a domino effect for the rest of the redevelopment district, where up to 1,300 residences, an entertainment center and some 600,000 square feet of commercial space are planned. A second round of negotiations with different developers for three areas in the district is now under way and the Applied Companies, of Hoboken, is the farthest along, not only in this round but since the redevelopment effort began, Mr. Woolley said.
In June, Applied, known for its residential redevelopment activity along the Hudson River waterfront, entered a memorandum of understanding with the city to redevelop most of the 25-acre Beachfront North site into up to 400 renovated town houses and rental apartments -- mostly new, but some renovated. There will also be a 20,000-square-foot commercial component, comprising new and existing stores on the site, between Madison and Seaview Avenues. Land between the project and the beach will be for recreational uses.
Details of the project are expected to be hammered out by the middle of next month at which time Applied would be designated the redeveloper of Beachfront North, said Mr. Woolley. Also being discussed is whether the city will provide tax abatement, a matter Applied intends to pursue.
Gregory S. Russo, a vice president of Applied, said the project is estimated to cost $50 million, including $8 million to buy the land. He said that construction was expected to start in six to nine months and that the project would be built over the next three years.
The intent of the Beachfront North project -- and by extension the redevelopment plan -- is to reposition Long Branch as a year-round community, not just a seasonal resort. It is also meant to inject new economic life into the city, which watched the tourism it relied on for its tax base erode in the 1950's when residents and then businesses fled for shore towns farther south.
There have been other attempts to revitalize the oceanfront, but each ran afoul of local politics, community opposition or burdensome approvals. The last, in the mid-80's, resulted in a spate of condominiums and a Hilton Hotel before the activity was derailed by cumbersome regulations. But even with both state and local beachfront regulations identical, thus obviating the need for approvals on both levels, the city has not consummated a development deal.
The plan can be modified "without destroying the integrity of what we want to accomplish, " said Carl Turner, the city's planning director. But some of the earlier developers wanted to modify it too much or asked for too much, which would have caused Long Branch to lose more than it would have gained, he said.
Ron Kaplowitz, a board member of Long Branch Tomorrow, added: "For those who are saying, 'What is taking so long?': It's a long process and a very valuable process when the art of compromise is tested, and the city is getting closer, it is not sitting back."
The International Resort Center, of Colorado Springs, Colo., last year bought the Hilton Hotel for $21 million and invested $10 million more to refurbish it as a conference center and resort. And just outside the southern edge of the redevelopment area developers have built or are building two residential projects with a total of 142 units.
The city's beachfront has been restored under a $20 million Federal program. Remnants of an old amusement pier destroyed by a 1987 fire have been razed by the city, which received a $125,000 state grant to study the feasibility of building a new pier for retail, entertainment or transportation uses, or a mix of them, said Mr. Woolley.
The city also acquired a vacant armory in the Beachfront North area from a developer who did not proceed with a plan to recycle it. Now the city is negotiating a deal with a new developer to raze the structure and replace it with a year-round beach and health club, Mr. Woolley said.
"It sends the message that the city is serious about the redevelopment plan," he said, adding that the city would soon sign memorandums of understanding with developers for two other projects.
One involves developing housing and shops next to the proposed pier. The other entails plans by the owners of the Oceanplace Conference Resort, the former Hilton hotel, to build a second tower and perhaps expand its conference and meeting rooms. And negotiations continue with the Advance Group of Bedminister to develop a retail center with up to 90,000 square feet of space.
The state has proposed new rules for development in the coastal region that would be aligned with its state Development and Redevelopment Plan, which encourages growth where highways and utilities are in place and discourages it in less developed areas.
REFERRING to the 1973 Coastal Area Facility Review Act, Judy Jengo, a deputy commissioner at the Department of Environmental Protection, said, "CAFRA is being updated to reflect smart growth." She said that the new rules would not only "make the process more predictable," but would also, for the first time, implement the principles of the state plan. Ms. Jengo said the new coastline development rules tied in with state guidelines on development were expected to be adopted by year's end.
But there is opposition from some legislators who successfully defeated two previous drafts of the regulations, as well as from the New Jersey Builders Association. "They won't work," said Patrick O'Keefe, the association's executive vice president. "They rely on an outdated and unimplemented state plan."
Whatever happens to state regulations, Long Branch's plan remains in place.
Mr. Russo, of Applied, said meeting state regulations under the city's plan was appealing and should "make the process quicker." But he said his company got involved with the Beachfront North project because "the ocean is an incredible draw," and it is an opportunity "to create summer and primary housing all at once."
As now planned, he said, the project would begin on the site's 15 empty acres and include some 300 housing units, 75 percent of them town houses and the rest rental apartments. The town houses will range in size from 1,500 to 2,200 square feet and in price from $225,000 to $440,000. Rents for apartments of 850 to 1,300 square feet will range from $900 to $1,800.
The hope, he said, is that the existing housing in the area improves on its own. But if that does not happen those units will renovated or replaced with new ones, bringing the number of units in the project to 400.
And what of the city's checkered development history? "We see enough here that is positive," Mr. Russo said, adding that the city "is also proactive."
Applied is also no stranger to urban redevelopment, having developed housing along the Hudson riverfront in Hoboken and Jersey City. Mr. Russo said the formula his company used in those cities would be used again in Long Branch: "Leverage the oceanfront views with the right product," he said, "and the demand will be there."

HEADLINE: In San Francisco, Developers Bet on High-Rises

BYLINE: By JOHN McCLOUD 

DATELINE: SAN FRANCISCO

 BODY:
TRADITIONALLY, a home with a view here has meant living on one of the many hills for which the city is celebrated. But a group of developers hopes to convince at least a few thousand residents that living on the upper floors of a contemporary glass and steel high-rise is as romantic and sophisticated as living in an Edwardian building atop Russian Hill or in Pacific Heights.
Judging by the response to Avalon Towers, a 16-story, 226-unit apartment complex that is the first high-rise apartment project to open in San Francisco in a decade, the developers' prospects look strong. According to Shelby Campbell, a development manager for the project's developers, AvalonBay Communities Inc. of Alexandria, Va., 146 units have rented since the two-building complex opened in July, with new leases being signed daily. 
 Five other residential high-rise complexes are in construction, with more in discussion. Developers of four of the other projects are from New York, which has led some observers to refer to the trend as Manhattanization. So far, the residential complexes have attracted minimal criticism, a rarity in this often-contentious city.
The projects with New York origins are a condominium complex, with two 17-story towers and one 16-story tower by the Reliance Development Group; a 40-story mixed hotel and condominium project by Millennium Partners; a 51-story mixed condominium and apartment tower by Swig-Burris Equities, and a 39-story apartment tower by the Related Companies. All but the Related project are under construction.
According to Kent Swig, a Swig-Burris partner and a former San Franciscan whose family owned the landmark Fairmont Hotel atop Nob Hill from the 1930's until last year, his company's $125 million project will be the tallest residential building west of the Mississippi.
The remaining two projects have no New York connection. The Monahan Pacific Construction Corporation of San Rafael, Calif., has a 13-story condominium building in construction, and Carpenter & Company of Cambridge, Mass., will build another 40-story combined hotel and condominium tower a block from the Millennium Group's.
All the new projects are clustered in the northeast section of an area known as "south of Market," which lies south of Market Street, the city's main downtown commercial corridor.
The burgeoning district is home to most of the software, multimedia and Internet companies that have quickly become a major force in the city's economy. One part of the district has earned the nickname Multimedia Gulch.
The area is also home to numerous shops, restaurants, clubs, museums, performance spaces and, most recently, a 360,000-square-foot entertainment complex with a 15-screen cinema.
A new stadium for the San Francisco Giants baseball team is in construction at the district's southern edge, as is a second campus for the University of California at San Francisco.
Until recently, the area held mostly warehouses, light industrial facilities and lower-rent office buildings left from the days when San Francisco was a major port. These older buildings became popular with startup high-technology ventures, which appreciated their ostensibly anticorporate esthetic. The influx of these companies has spurred conversion of substantially more than a million square feet of vacant or underutilized properties to offices with annual rents of $40 a square foot.
Alan Mark, president of the Mark Company and sales director for the Reliance Group project, Brannan Place, calls the intense concentration a major selling point for residential development.
"A lot of people are working 60 hours a week." he said. "When they get home, they want to walk to everything. This is the only place in the city you can do that."
Mr. Mark is confident that Brannan Place will sell quickly, despite prices of $500 a square foot for units averaging 1,150 square feet. San Francisco's homebuying market is extremely tight and buyers appear willing to pay what it costs to get in.
The rental market is equally tight. According to most real estate companies, the city's apartment vacancy rate has hovered around 1 percent for 18 months and rents have risen dramatically. Rents at Avalon Towers range from $2,400 to $5,400 for one- and two-bedroom units of 800 to 1,650 square feet.
The residents of the new projects will not represent a typical cross section of San Francisco. At Avalon Towers, Ms. Campbell says, 85 percent of the tenants earn more than $100,000 a year. Nearly three-quarters are single or divorced and 60 percent are under 40. Two-thirds are new to the city.
Mark Levy, a real estate developer who moved here from Silicon Valley following a divorce, says he chose his two-bedroom unit because of its location in the heart of the city and for the amenities and views. Avalon Towers has a largely unobstructed view of San Francisco Bay from three sides and of the city's western hills from the fourth.
Mr. Levy, who relocated to California from Stamford, Conn., a year ago, says he likes the project's cosmopolitan image. "I quickly determined I didn't want old, didn't want ground floor, didn't want walkup," he said, adding, "It's simple, easy, free living."
Mr. Swig is counting on people like Mr. Levy for his project, which will have 335 rental apartments and 174 condominiums. Though rents and prices have not yet been set, he says the building, called the Century and scheduled for completion in 2001, is aimed at the upper end of the market.
According to Dan Murphy, an AvalonBay vice president, most of the projects represent a response to market demand rather than explicit city policy, though the Millennium, Related and Carpenter developments are in an official redevelopment zone and are partly funded with tax-exempt bonds issued by the city's Redevelopment Agency.
City officials, however, have expressed support for all the projects and are taking steps to encourage more of them. Susanna Montana, a senior planner for the city, said that the Planning Department wanted to raise height limits on Rincon Hill, a 12-block area that includes Avalon Towers, to 350 feet from the current 250 to encourage development.
"We asked architects representing prospective buyers what they want to build and we asked property owners, workers and current residents what kind of area they would like to see," she said. "By and large, the people who are there every day want a 24-hour neighborhood. To get that, we need to get more people in there, and that means going up."
Whether San Franciscans will take to high-rise living in numbers sufficient to spur additional development remains to be seen, but Mr. Murphy is confident that they will.
"San Francisco is a world-class city, and there's a lot of people who would like to live here but haven't found the right type of place," he said. "I believe the market is pretty deep,"
Mr. Swig agrees. "I think the need is absolutely there," he said. "With the right context and right design, high-rise living is a wonderful way of life. It's not for everybody, but I think San Francisco needs it to attract the kind of people who will keep the economy moving forward in the 21st century."

HEADLINE: Amid Boom, Jobs in Retailing Slip Away

BYLINE: By STEWART AIN 

 BODY:
AT a time when the Long Island economy is humming along -- sales tax revenue in the first seven months of this year was up 5.3 percent and the median price of homes in Nassau soared last year by 13.6 percent -- retail jobs are dwindling.
"Although Long Island gained 26,700 jobs overall in the 12 months ending in July, it lost 200 retail jobs during that period," said Pearl Kamer, chief economist of the Long Island Association, the Island's largest business organization. 
 In fact, the retail sector has steadily been losing jobs since 1988, the year before a major recession struck Long Island, according to Gary Huth, labor market analyst for the New York State Department of Labor's Long Island region.
"There was a sense on Long Island that jobs in the retail trade were the leading component in the job recovery," he said. "But the share of jobs in the retail trade is actually edging downward."
He said that in 1998, 18.3 percent of all jobs on Long Island were in retailing, compared with 1988, when they represented 19.2 percent of the job total. Not only did the retail sector not keep pace with the rest of the Island's economy, but the number of retail jobs actually dropped by 8,000 -- from 218,400 in 1988 to 210,400 last year.
Mr. Huth said there were 1,149,100 jobs on Long Island in 1998 -- 12,000 more than 1988.
"The economy has been percolating," he said. "That was a 1 percent increase in jobs in 10 years. At the same time, there has been a 3.7 percent drop in overall retail jobs."
Mr. Huth expressed surprise at the figures, saying they indicated that "Long Island retailers must be finding a more productive business model for achieving sales."
"There must be more sales per employee," he said. "Long Island is leading the nation in that area."
Those figures also came as a surprise to Bruce W. VanKleeck, vice president of member services of the National Retail Federation in Washington, who said that the "overall growth in jobs nationally has been in retail jobs."
"The retail sector employs 20 percent of U.S. workers, and industry sales last year were $2.7 trillion," he said. "I'm surprised to hear that on Long Island, with its high per capita ratios, retail jobs have not been accelerating."
Asked if the figures might indicate a saturation of retail businesses, Mr. VanKleeck said that while critics might say so, "as long as consumers spend disposable income, there is business to be had."
Ms. Kamer of the Long Island Association said sales taxes on Long Island, which are used as a proxy for retail sales, showed that Long Islanders were spending.
"If you look at the year-to-year increases, you will see that in April the increase in sales tax revenues in Nassau and Suffolk was 13.8 percent," she said. "If you look at March to March, it was 9.1 percent."
As in other years, Ms. Kamer said, sales slowed in June by 1.6 percent, but "came up nicely in July, increasing by 6.4 percent." She said they were expected to be strong in August and September to reflect back-to-school buying.
"Consumer purchasing power is strong," she said, adding that it was a reflection of rising salaries and a strong stock market.
"A lot of Long Islanders own stocks, which gives them an incentive to spend more freely," said Ms. Kamer. "If they don't cash them in, it is paper wealth, but people believe they are wealthier."
There are other Long Islanders who are actually wealthier because they have sold their homes, "which have greatly escalated in price in the last year or two," she pointed out. "The median sales price of homes in Nassau rose from $180,000 in 1997 to $204,500 in 1998 -- 13.6 percent. In Suffolk, it increased from $145,000 to $149,000, a 2.8 percent increase. When we get the figures for June, we'll see an even greater jump because demand for homes exceeds inventory. So this is motivating people to spend."
Ms. Kamer observed that retail sales were up an average of 5.3 percent in the first seven months of this year, "which is very, very good considering that the inflation rate in the region is 2.1 percent."
"But that doesn't explain why retail employment is down 200 jobs," she said. "My theory is that retailers are trying to remain competitive and improve their profit margins by keeping their labor costs as low as possible."
She said that in general, discount stores have done the best on Long Island while department stores with moderately priced apparel have not done as well.
"There is a lot of purchasing power on Long Island, but there are a lot of retailers," she said. "It's very competitive here. You also have big box retailers competing with each other, such as the Home Depots, Staples and BJ's. They are competing on price and learning to control inventory well. There are many analysts who believe that even for the level of affluence here there are too many retailers selling the same or similar products. And when the economy turns down, as inevitably it does because we have a cyclical economy, there will be some degree of shake-out among the retailers."
Harold J. Bordwin, vice president of Keen Realty Consultants in Great Neck, said the "competition in the retail industry is so intense that there is an incredible amount of pressure on retailers to do everything absolutely right."
He said Caldor, a department store that went out of business earlier this year, failed because it was not able to generate the sales it needed.
"And the competition is only getting worse with the Internet," Mr. Bordwin said. "How much time do people want to spend shopping for items in a store when they can do it from their homes or even from television?"
But he said people would still go to the store for the more expensive items because they "want to see and feel them before they spend their money."
Malachy Kavanagh, a spokesman for the International Council of Shopping Centers in Manhattan, pointed out that Caldor had intense competition from Wal-Mart, Kmart, and Target stores. And no sooner did Caldor vacate the stores, than K-Mart and another chain, Kohl's, bought them.
"In retailing, the strongest survives," he said. "You could make the argument that there are too many stores, but too many is good for the consumer because it drives prices down. Just look at supermarkets that are close to each other and the battles they have with prices. Who benefits? The consumer."
Richard A. Rauch, a marketing professor at the C. W. Post Campus of Long Island University, said that supermarkets are doing "very, very poorly" as a result of competition. "To keep up their volume, they are cutting prices and doubling coupons," he said. "It's good for the consumer, but they are not making any money. That's true of Edwards, Waldbaum's and Pathmark."
He pointed out that Ahold, a Netherlands company that owns Edwards and Stop & Shop, recently bought Pathmark.
Among the most successful stores on Long Island are those that discount prices, such as those in the outlet centers in Riverhead and Bellport, Ms. Kamer noted.
"Long Islanders are attached to a bargain," she said. "The deep recession we had in the early 1990's contributed to that mentality and it never left. People here shop based on price and quality, but price may be first for many consumers."

HEADLINE: JERSEY;
2 Machines Are Needed: One for Time, One for Cash

BYLINE: By NEIL GENZLINGER 

 BODY:
END-OF-SUMMER Points to Ponder: 1) You can learn from your parents, and you can learn from the past, but can you learn from your parents' past? 2) Shouldn't this country be devoting all its resources to developing a workable time machine?
Let me explain. Our household has just played host to Amy, 13, who flew here from Wisconsin without adult accompaniment to visit my daughter Emily. By coincidence, my mother recently gave me a scrapbook from her own teen-age cross-country journey, made 60 years ago, also without adult accompaniment. 
 The two trips had their differences, of course. Mom traveled by train, since airplanes were still falling out of the sky on a regular basis in 1939. Also, she went east to west, from Philadelphia to California, and she was 19, not 13. Nonetheless, there are plenty of points of comparison between the trips, all of them pretty depressing.
For instance, the camaraderie of the road isn't what it used to be. Today, transportation workers and fellow travelers are something to be feared. Sixty years ago, they were partners in an adventure.
"There was a girl across the aisle who looked tired and bored," my mother wrote from the train, "so I wrote a note asking if she wanted someone to talk to, made it into a dart and sailed it across to her. She seemed pleased to have company and we got along famously." Amy threw no paper darts at fellow airplane passengers, but recent unpleasantness at Newark Airport tells us what would have happened if she had: "Dozens Injured as Child's Note Ignites Body-Slamming Fracas on Jet."
And what exactly has happened to celebrities in the last 60 years? Mom got into Paramount Studios, met Leslie Howard, had her picture taken with Jackie Cooper. When she ate lunch, Dorothy Lamour was at the next table.
Here in cruel 1999, I took Amy and Emily to a Yankees game. Afterward we waited an hour at the players' exit, hoping for a memorable moment. All we got was a glimpse of a couple of custom-made suits as the heartless heroes galloped onto the team bus without so much as a wave.
Even luck was better in 1939. In San Francisco, Mom and the fellow she had gone west to see, now known in our family as Dad, took in the Golden Gate International Exposition. Bell Telephone had an exhibit there offering free long-distance calls, a novelty at the time, to people who won a lucky-number drawing. It took her three trips to the exhibit, but Mom won the call, using it, naturally, to phone home.
Luck, however, has now been replaced by perverse fickleness, which is why I'm not driving a new motorcycle. Amy arrived from Wisconsin bearing gifts; I got a Harley-Davidson T-shirt, since the company is in Milwaukee. A few days later I'm wearing the shirt when I pop into a dry cleaner's to drop something off. There, in the corner, is the last thing one expects to see in a dry cleaner's: a lottery terminal. And a new scratch-off game is being promoted: "Ticket to Ride." Top prize: a Harley.
Why New Jersey is selling chances on Wisconsin motorcycles is unclear, but I know fate when I see it -- or I thought I did. I bought three tickets and flipped my car keys to the guy behind the counter. "It's all yours, pal," I told him. "Four-wheelers are for squares." I realize now that I should have scratched the tickets first.
Lastly, there is, of course, the financial comparison. For our three Yankees tickets I paid $87, roughly the cost of Mom's train fare from Philadelphia to San Francisco to Los Angeles and back. On the train, Mom could buy an elaborate dinner for 35 cents. After the Yankees game, we stopped at New York's Jekyll and Hyde Club and asked if they'd sell us dinner for 35 cents; once they stopped laughing, they sold us three Cokes for almost $10. A few days later we paid more to rent bowling shoes at Carolier Lanes in New Brunswick ($6) than Mom paid for . . . well, for practically anything.
Economists will say, "You're forgetting about inflation," but that's not the point. The point is, we need the time machine mentioned in the first paragraph. Imagine what we could do if we took a fistful of 1999 dollars back to 1939. For what Amy's trip cost, Mom could have made a lunar landing. For what the Yanks pay Clemens, we could have built a dozen more Hudson River crossings. For what we paid to bowl two games, we could have bought bowling shoes for every man, woman and child in New Jersey. Woulda looked great with the Harley-Davidson T-shirts.
 
http://www.nytimes.com

HEADLINE: DESTINATIONS;
Smiling Cheese and Other Good Food

BYLINE: By JOSEPH D'AGNESE 

DATELINE: COLTS NECK

 BODY:
AN impatient dad tried to coax his toddler daughter into tasting a free sample from the cheese table at Delicious Orchards in Colts Neck. She pushed away his meager offering -- a tiny cube of white cheddar -- and lunged instead for a block of white and yellow mild cheddar molded into a smiley face.
Dad eventually gave in, dropping the Lancashire-and-Red Leicester cheese combo into his shopping cart. 
 Most people who flock to this local institution know what they're in for. It's the kind of place that lures customers with cookies and coffee, where old-fashioned wooden signs promise pies, breads and 40-cent doughnuts. Unsliced loaves of bread recline on red-and-white picnic napkins in wicker baskets, and cartons of cranberry juice and apple cider are never far from the potato bins.
In an inviting 25,000-square-foot retail space that resembles an oversize barn, shoppers can buy everything from fresh meats and chocolates to coffee beans, gift baskets and impeccably fresh produce.
It's hard to resist the charm. Delicious Orchards may be nestled on the grounds of a nearly 90-year-old apple orchard, but its managers and 300 employees run the place with modern-day savvy. Need a gift basket of fruit, apple crumb cake and brownies sent overnight anywhere in the United States? Operators are standing by. Need a prepared meal in a hurry? Good, reheatable meals -- like chicken francese with garlic string bean salad, asparagus Milanese and garlic mashed potatoes -- are waiting behind the prepared foods counter.
Everything is carefully considered, down to the smiling cheeses.
"Aren't those adorable?" said Cynthia Nappa, the specialty foods manager, who selects, among other things, the 400 or so cheeses sold here. "It's a nice way to introduce kids to cheese that's good for them." When was the last time a supermarket worried about developing your child's palate?
In that respect, Delicious Orchards is a market with a mission: to help redefine the way suburban families think about and shop for food.
Its use of mail-order catalogues and aggressive advertising campaigns have made its allure felt well outside Monmouth County. Nearly 2.5 million people walked through its doors last year alone, many of them driving over the Verrazano-Narrows Bridge to see what all the fuss was about. They learned what locals have long known: buy iceberg lettuce at your local supermarket, if you must, but when you want the good stuff, go to Delicious.
In many ways, this is a story of what's old is what's new. The orchard itself was 11 years old when the Barclay family bought it in 1922, a time when local produce was routinely barged up the Navesink River to Sandy Hook, bound for Manhattan fruit stalls. That changed in the late 1950's, when Carroll Barclay, son of the original owners, started selling the family's apples and peaches, along with cider and baked goods, directly to consumers.
His wife, Janet, reigned in the bakery. "My mother was tough," recalled David Barclay, 50, who runs the orchard. "It had to be right, or we didn't do it at all. The quality had to be top-notch, and the customer was always right."
By 1989, everything but the orchard had been sold to a new generation of owners: three employees who, though unrelated to the family, seem determined to do things the Barclay way. "We want you to feel good from the moment you walk in to the moment you leave," said Thomas J. Gesualdo, the company's silver-haired president, who began working for the Barclays in 1974. "In many ways, we have it easier than the big chain supermarkets. We stick primarily to produce and the bakery. Everything else supports that."
It helps that Mr. Gesualdo's two partners have considerable experience. One, Frank McMahon, a baker for 27 years, began working for Mrs. Barclay when he was 20. Today, he has assumed her role in the bakery, agonizing over whether to raise the price of pies (the most expensive is $8.75), insisting on cleanliness and shepherding 55 employees through the daily routine of cranking out thousands of baked goods.
In an average day, the bakery produces 500 pies. Last Thanksgiving week, the bakery made and sold 65,000 pies, a number Mr. McMahon still shudders to think about. The bakery shifts begin at midnight. By the time customers arrive at 10 A.M., they can choose from 10 different kinds of pies, 11 breads, four types of doughnuts and legions of small pastries, cakes, tarts and brownies -- all baked according to recipes conceived by Mrs. Barclay in the 1950's.
The produce manager, Bill McDonald, has a similar story. He began stocking shelves here as an 18-year-old. Today, as a co-owner, he enforces the company's three-tiered system for quality control: the produce is inspected first by the buyers, then by the company's truck drivers and finally by employees at the store.
The company says it makes an effort to buy New Jersey produce in season, provided the food makes the grade. "We have an agreement with everyone we buy from," Mr. McDonald said. "It must meet our standards. If we don't like a load of oranges, we just reject it. That's it."
Shoppers seem to notice the difference. "I buy fruits and vegetables from them, and nothing gets wasted," said Hope Ruetiman of Freehold, who was heading inside the store one afternoon. "Other stores, you buy food and it goes bad a few days later. That never happens here. It's good quality."
Her son, Harrison, 9, couldn't wait to get inside to tackle the samples. His favorite snack? "Pistachios!" he blurted. His playmate, Alison Frick, 8, shyly confessed her weakness: cantaloupe.
A supermarket that encourages children to eat well may be on to something. But it's all in keeping with the spirit of the place. Shoppers here don't just shop; they snack. On a recent weekday, two elderly gentlemen popped plump red grapes in their mouths as they chatted by the bins. At the meat and deli counter, a man offered his wife some spiced roast beef on waxed paper.
Nearby, another man waited for his wife, minding their shopping cart and her purse while nibbling at a plate of cole slaw. If the salads aren't sold by the end of a day, Ms. Nappa said, they are offered as free samples the next.
Mrs. Barclay, who died five years ago at 69, would have approved of such economy. In fact, her son says, she would be proud of Delicious Orchards, which today retains much of the spirit and bustle of her original business. 'There was always a lot of people and excitement there," Mr. Barclay recalled. 'We were always surrounded by success, and it's still going on. It's this little market that grew into a massive place."
 
Delicious Orchards, Route 34, Colts Neck. Hours: Tuesdays through Sundays, 10 A.M. to 6 P.M. (732) 462-1989. Mail-order catalogue: (800) 624-1893.

HEADLINE: ART REVIEW;
Peripatetic Project Takes Root

BYLINE: By WILLIAM ZIMMER 

DATELINE: JERSEY CITY

 BODY:
THREE years after Robert Costa conceived of his public sculpture project "Art on the Waterfront," he installed it in Peninsula Park downtown in July. Mr. Costa is especially pleased that the same artists he chose back then are featured now, on the site he originally wanted.
The state denied him permission at the time. Hoboken was poised to take the exhibition for its waterfront this summer. Publicity had gone out and advertisements had been placed, but that plan was ultimately blocked by two City Council members. 
 "There are real differences between Hoboken and Jersey City, and they go back several generations," said Mr. Costa, a freelance curator affiliated with Pro Arts, which puts art in public spaces. "Hoboken had a lot of speak-easies during Prohibition, and it's still a bar culture. Music flourishes, but the visual arts have always had a hard time there. On the other hand, Jersey City has a strong reputation as a place friendly to art, and City Hall helped us get this site."
It's obvious that in selecting specific pieces, Mr. Costa has paid close attention to the particulars of Peninsula Park, at the entrance to the historic Morris Canal. The sculptures at the back of the site are relatively large and hold a kind of dialogue with the skyline of lower Manhattan, a de facto feature of the park. Smaller pieces in front refer more to the water.
"Giunta," by Carole Eisner, is named for the trucker who helps her transport her heavy welded-steel work. The abstract assemblage sculpture is all rust-colored metal, but some of the components are rough, both in texture and contour, while others are smooth. It's easy to see these latter elements as urbane, a reflection of the idealized life style across the Hudson in Manhattan; the more rugged elements might refer to the rather untamed look of Peninsula Park.
Nearby is a precarious-looking cinder-block "Tower," by Stefanie Nagorka. "When she showed me her proposal, it was done with sugar cubes," Mr. Costa said. "There's no mortar between the blocks. Instead, the blocks, which aren't flush with each other, are kept together by an armature." Visually, the fragile tower contrasts with the stable skyline across the river, but high finance, the main business of lower Manhattan and nearby Exchange Place in Jersey City, has its own kind of fragility.
A near relation of the tower is a blocky concrete figure by Joseph Chirchirillo that seems to be on its knees in a vulnerable attitude, or perhaps one of reverence toward New York City. Mr. Chirchirillo calls his figure "Leaner," and if Ms. Nagorka's piece isn't really Pisa-like, this one makes up for it, as a foil to the ramrod-straight architecture it faces.
Another in the world of economical titles is Howard McCalebb's "Calypso." It might be viewed as a transitional work: relatively monumental in scale, like the previously discussed work, but also having a watery pedigree. It might come under the heading of what are called "drawings in space," sculpture heavily dependent on the pattern made by a metal line. Mr. McCalebb has included a large coiled spring, which Mr. Costa says relates the work to sprightly calypso music. The title may also refer to the sea nymph in "The Odyssey," even though the Morris Canal entrance is hardly the deep blue sea. But reminding the viewer of water's potential depth is an addition that might help "Calypso" sink to the bottom: a bowling ball.
"Involute," a welded bronze circle by Thomas Beckmann, might be a water wheel. Each unit ends in a splayed-out shape resembling a spoon that might temporarily catch liquid, but a closer relative would be a windmill, and "Involute" rotates in the breeze. "Three Seahorses," by Luis A. Pratts, has diverse local references. Mr. Costa says actual seahorses have been found in the waters surrounding Peninsula Park, so the work pays tribute to the ecology of the place. The animals' intricate bony plates are made of railroad pins, bent and welded together; they are abundant in the area of the park that used to be laid with track. "Three Seahorses" is also a homage to bygone industrial life.
Steven Rand has his own titling system, so the viewer can't associate "6NHL" with anything in the work. It looks like a fat python coiled in the dry grass. The work is sections of sewage pipe fitted together with bright metal sleeves that conceal the seams between the sections. Around Peninsula Park, a visitor can spot all manner of washed-out objects, so the hunkering waste pipe is a recognition of this less-than-elegant facet of the site.
The work perched closest to the water is Lillian Ball's "Hot Tub," cast in urethane foam rubber and painted a swimming-pool blue. The work strongly suggests a flying saucer that has landed, and it is oddly shaped because what's cast is not the tub, but the space the water would take up. One of the pleasures of the piece is feeling its surface yield to the touch; the first thought is that it is concrete. Hot tubs are hedonistic, and Ms. Ball's work, which suggests a couch or a love seat, is being used that way.
"Art on the Waterfront" is scheduled to close on Dec. 5, when, the practical-minded Mr. Costa says, the insurance runs out.
 
ART ON THE WATERFRONT
Peninsula Park, on the Morris Canal
by Portside Tower East at Washington Street, Jersey City
Through Dec. 5. Hours: Daily, 6 A.M. to 10 P.M.

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
Report Ranks Newark Worst City for Children

BYLINE: By KAREN DeMASTERS 

 BODY:
Newark was ranked the worst place to rear children out of 112 medium-sized cities in the nation in a report released last week by Zero Population Growth, a Washington-based nonprofit organization created to educate the public about the impact of rapid population growth.
The report divided metropolitan areas into three types: major cities; independent cities, which have more than 100,000 residents and are not part of the sphere of a major city, and suburbs or outer cities, which are cities with more than 100,000 population that are part of another major metropolitan area. 
 Elizabeth, Paterson and Jersey City are considered suburbs of New York City by Zero Population Growth, and all ranked in the bottom third of the outer cities category. Out of 92 suburbs, Elizabeth ranked 75th, Paterson 86th, and Jersey City 89th. San Bernardino, Calif., came in last of the suburbs.
The rankings were based on nearly a dozen criteria, with the teen-age birth rate, number of children living in poverty, dropout rate, unemployment rate and violent crime rate being the most important factors, the report said. KAREN DeMASTERS

HEADLINE: JERSEY FOOTLIGHTS;
The Fine Art of Cabinetmaking

BYLINE: By DIANE NOTTLE 

 BODY:
A lot of artisans think their work is museum-quality. John Hein of Hopewell knows his is: the Newark Museum has told him so, in the nicest possible way.
 In July, the museum was given Mr. Hein's "Coopered Cabinet," completed in 1998, by Robert Alonzo Winters of Hightstown, a retired economics professor at Rutgers University who collects his work. It is now on display in the museum's House and Home Galleries. 
 The cabinet, 69 inches tall, is made of walnut, Honduras rosewood, pearwood, applewood and bloodwood. "It's something of an experimental piece," Mr. Hein said. Several elements presented challenges, particularly the curved door; he consulted a Princeton University physics professor about hinges that would let it swing open properly. No wonder the project -- "including all the mistakes" -- took him an estimated 500 hours over two years.
 Mr. Hein, 44, who graduated from Watchung Hills Regional High School, majored in English at Temple University, but in his craft he is largely self-taught. He must be a good teacher: he is one of only three living Americans in the Design Encyclopedia's list of eminent furniture designers, which puts him in the company of figures like Thomas Chippendale, Frank Lloyd Wright and a fellow New Jerseyan, Gustav Stickley. His pieces sell for $3,000 to $20,000. DIANE NOTTLE

HEADLINE: IN BRIEF;
Sound's Guardian

BYLINE: By ELSA BRENNER 

 BODY:
County Executive Andrew J. Spano will soon be the recipient of the second yearly Sound Guardian Award. A coalition of environmentalists and construction companies, which has worked for six years to fix problems in Long Island Sound, will honor Mr. Spano next month for his efforts to do the same. Gov. George E. Pataki won the first award last year for his effort to clean the environment. 
 The National Audubon Society of New York and the Construction Industry Council of Westchester and Hudson Valley will present the award. In the early 1990's, the two organizations founded the Clean Water Jobs Coalition to promote financing for cleaning the Sound. It was the first time in Westchester that industry, labor and environmental organizations united for a common goal. The groups realized that upgrades at sewer plants, for example, not only benefited the environment but also provided jobs and stimulated the economy.
David J. Miller, executive director of National Audubon of New York, said proceeds from a $150-a-plate dinner for Mr. Spano on Sept. 8 will be used to continue those efforts. Mr. Spano has overseen upgrades at the county's seven sewage-treatment plants, including the four on the Sound.
The county has also underwritten projects to remove nitrogen from sewage treated at the Blind Brook plant in Rye and the Mamaroneck sewage treatment plant.
Nitrogen, a major component of sewage, has encouraged algae growth in the Sound, which in turn led to fish kills. Mr. Spano's administration has also focused its efforts on restoring and preserving fish and wildlife habitats on the estuary's shoreline.

HEADLINE: NEW YORKERS & CO.;
The Discreet Charm of the Mystery Bar

BYLINE: By ERIC V. COPAGE 

 BODY:
THE phone rang and Kenny Eybs, a bartender at an East Village bar, picked it up and prepared to go through a familiar drill.
"Hello, the Musical Box," he said. 
 "I can't find you," said a weary voice from a cell phone. "I've been walking up and down the block for 20 minutes!"
"Where are you?" Mr. Eybs asked with practiced calm. "Now turn around," he continued. "Walk up to the door. Now pull."
The Musical Box is a mystery bar, one of those establishments that do not advertise with neon lights or gaudy signs or large windows through which happy multitudes float about like fish in an aquarium. While such hidden bars are not new, many have sprung up in recent years, said Clark Wolf, an independent food and restaurant consultant based in New York.
"It is absolutely on the upswing," said Mr. Wolf, calling mystery bars "one of the biggest traditions of New York, going back to the speakeasy." Their growth "tends to happen during periods of economic upturn," he added, when night life is hopping and customers plentiful -- as they are now.
These bars opt for varying degrees of anonymity. WCOU, commonly called "The Tile Bar" because of its tiled exterior, has no sign, but its presence is clear from its big uncurtained windows, which front onto a busy corner of First Avenue and Seventh Street in the East Village.
Some mystery bars do have signs, but discreet ones. Descending the steps into Pravda, on Lafayette Street in SoHo, you see a tiny rectangle of illumination with the bar's name. Torch, a bar and restaurant on Ludlow Street on the Lower East Side, has its name in brass embedded in cement in front. Sway, which opened in December at 305 Spring Street, simply has the wrong sign. It says "McGovern's Bar."
Other establishments have only a symbol in front. The Temple Bar, also on Lafayette Street, has a skeleton of a chameleon, for instance.
But the most mysterious bars are those without any signs, symbols or other identifying traits. One would not suspect they were there at all. To get to the Lansky Lounge, on Norfolk Street on the Lower East Side, for example, patrons must travel a seemingly dicey alley to get to a door without a name.
What is the lure of such spots?
Anthony Secunda, a comic who celebrated his birthday recently at the Lansky Lounge, said, "If people are afraid to go in or can't find it, I don't have to worry about tourists overrunning the place."
Such obscurity can turn such a place into a neighborhood bar of sorts, even if its patrons live in such distant locations as the Upper West Side, the Upper East Side and Williamsburg.
Lauren Feeney, who was having drinks recently at another mystery establishment, the three-year-old Orchard Bar on the Lower East Side, said she found such bars "cool and underground" when she arrived here a year ago. Now she considers them "almost too trendy," but still frequents them.
"You feel you can talk to the people next to you -- or not," said Ms. Feeney, 22, a listings editor for The New York Review of Books. "In a way you automatically have something in common with them because you both sought out this obscure place."
Mr. Wolf, the restaurant consultant, confirms these patrons' feeling of clubbiness, saying, "When it's hard to find, you feel like you own it." The business result, he said, is a stable clientele.
"As opposed to super-trendy places where the fashionable crowd comes and goes, or the obvious-location places where you just gather the streams of pedestrians, these are places people choose to go and to go back to," he said.
Moreover, pulling open the doors to a mystery bar is oddly exhilarating, like finding an egg during an Easter egg hunt. "It creates an energy, a tension, regardless of what you offer once they are inside," said Peter Kane, a co-owner of the Orchard Bar and of the year-old Double Happiness on Mott Street in Chinatown.
Still, mystery is not everything. "Some people think that not having a name is a formula for success," Mr. Kane said. "But it is the vibe you create, not just making the place difficult to find."
Some proprietors also say the mystery is mere happenstance. Allison Culbertson, co-owner of Void, an art bar and performance space tucked away on Mercer Street in an obscure corner of SoHo, said: "It's not that we planned to be hidden. We simply fell in love with the location."
Moreover, "We didn't want to attract a lot of attention," Ms. Culbertson said of her four-year-old establishment, which has a small black sign about eight feet high on the corner of her building. "This is not a meat market or a pretentious SoHo bar. This is just a place where people can chill."
Johnny Barounis, who owns three signless spots, added that mystery bars are mainly a Manhattan phenomenon.
"With the Victorian Lounge in Bayside, we wanted to do the exact thing we did in Manhattan," said Mr. Barounis, whose bars include the two-year-old Exile on West 70th Street and the two-and-a-half-year-old Musical Box, which is on Avenue B between 13th and 14th Streets. "But they didn't get it. We put up two signs and had a valet and still people would say they didn't know it was there."
He continued: "I've been to other cities and you don't get a lot of signless bars outside of New York. Part of the reason is that people are driving to the location, and if you miss the place you can be 20 miles away from it in the blink of an eye."
Moreover, the "hip" factor in nameless, faceless bars and the resulting media interest make it difficult for them to stay mysteries for long. "You have people whose job it is to find out about these places and write about them," Mr. Kane said. "You've heard of six degrees of separation. It's more like four degrees before anyone in New York knows someone in the media. Even if you wanted to be underground and successful, you couldn't stay underground for long."
In this hot real estate market, the devouring of abandoned buildings and industrial areas also works against these bars' secrecy. "Everything is becoming residential," Mr. Barounis said.
Of course, some people -- even patrons -- dislike certain aspects of these mystery bars. "I find walking down a weird dark alleyway unappealing," said Sean Conroy, 32, a comic and writer who recently celebrated his birthday at Lansky Lounge.
Mr. Secunda agreed. "The feeling that I am going to get mugged is not all that pleasant," he said.
Indeed, some establishments wind up chucking all the mystery. On the final rounds of a mazy late night searching for nameless, faceless bars, a reporter was surprised by a big sign hanging over the street and identifying Dharma, a bar that had been unmarked. Inside, the bartender was idly conversing with a patron. The reporter asked: Wasn't Dharma, at 174 Orchard Street on the Lower East Side, suppose to be one of Manhattan's mystery bars?
"We were," the bartender said. "But we finally had to put one up. People kept complaining that they couldn't find us."
 
http://www.nytimes.com

GRAPHIC: Photo: Double Happiness has been open for a year on Mott Street in Chinatown. A co-owner, Peter Kane, says opening the door of a mystery bar "creates an energy, a tension." (Frances Roberts for The New York Times)
 
Chart: "DIRECTORY: Bring Your Cell Phone"
Manhattan has many mystery bars -- watering holes tucked into obscure corners or sporting hard-to-see signs, if any at all. If the address is not enough to locate the spots, call.
