SECTION: Section A; Page 1; Column 4; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 4049 words

HEADLINE: THE LAST LANDFILL: A special report.;
As Deadline Looms for Dump, Alternate Plan Proves Elusive

BYLINE: By DOUGLAS MARTIN and ANDREW C. REVKIN 

 BODY:
PUB-DATE: May 29, 1996,
There was one small problem. 
 Until 48 hours before the announcement, no one in the Mayor's office had bothered to tell the Sanitation Department about the timetable: that after Dec. 31, 2001, not a single old shoe, not a single "I <heart> New York" coffee cup, not a single piece of rotting refuse would ever again be buried at Fresh Kills. No exceptions.
The department suddenly had to figure out what to do with the 13,000 tons of household garbage added each day to the artificial mountain. Only a few months earlier, sanitation officials were still counting on using Fresh Kills for 15 more years or so.
Jaws dropped and expletives flowed, one senior sanitation official said. The challenge, another official said, "seemed like skiing down a slope with a lot of trees, on one leg." Nevertheless, the politicians' pledge, bolstered by a state law that passed a week later, guaranteed that after nearly 50 years of taking trash to the dump and covering it with dirt, there had to be a new plan.
Thus began a long-running, and still unfinished, scramble that has made New York City the butt of jokes about trading culture for trash and has produced sharp divisions among and within the boroughs over the growing burdens they will have to bear.
Just as critically, it has provided a telling insight into the political clout of Staten Island and its Borough President, Guy V. Molinari, a lifelong Republican whose constituents helped insure the electoral victories of the Mayor and the Governor.
Even now, with just over two years to go, sanitation officials still do not know where or how they will dispose of most of the city's residential garbage.
The city's latest trash management plan, written after the 1996 announcement and updated since then, assumes that all of the garbage can flow to distant landfills smoothly by rail, truck or barge. But almost every route faces political and legal challenges.
"Everyone agrees closing Fresh Kills makes sense, but it is wrong to set a date before you have a realistic plan," said Eric A. Goldstein, a lawyer at the Natural Resources Defense Council, a private environmental group based in Washington.
Few people would dispute that Fresh Kills is an environmental nightmare. Designed when even the most dangerous substances were routinely dumped in unlined pits, the landfill has for five decades befouled the air of western Staten Island with noxious gases and has polluted the ground water with cyanide, pesticides and toxic waste.
But interviews with dozens of current and former city and state officials and executives of the nation's top waste-hauling companies suggest that plans for closing it -- and the timetable that Mr. Giuliani dictated -- have been shaped more by politics than by any thorough analysis of the city's needs and options.
"The Mayor has yet to involve the leaders of all five boroughs to develop a rational plan for the city's waste," said Howard Golden, the Borough President of Brooklyn, whose trash burden is almost certain to grow under whatever plan is devised. "The Mayor's current policy is a reward to his supporters on Staten Island."
Mayor Giuliani denies any political motivation, saying the issue is fairness. "Residents of the other four boroughs owe the people of Staten Island a debt of gratitude for what they have endured over the last half-century," he said recently. "The city should have closed the landfill years ago, and it's time to correct that mistake. It's not favoritism. It's simply the right thing to do."
Joseph J. Lhota, the Deputy Mayor for Operations, said he was confident that Fresh Kills would close on time and pointed to various interim steps, like exporting household trash from the Bronx and Brooklyn to out-of-state landfills, that have cut the daily flow to Fresh Kills to 8,500 tons, from 13,000. Even without a long-term solution, he said, the city will have plenty of short-term options.
The likeliest would be to send 600 sanitation trucks, each lugging nine tons of trash, in daily caravans through the already traffic-clogged Holland and Lincoln Tunnels to depots in New Jersey and elsewhere. In addition, at least 240 18-wheelers crammed with garbage would head directly to out-of-town landfills.
But even those interim steps are likely to leave some trash without a home, according to waste industry officials and lawyers tracking the plans.
And sanitation officials this summer disclosed that if interim plans become bogged down in legal battles, they will need to invoke "emergency powers" to keep the garbage moving, hiring even bigger fleets of private trucks -- without environmental reviews or community hearings -- to clear streets of trash to prevent disease.
Meanwhile, the city's latest long-term plan -- which calls for building two giant, privately run waste transfer stations on the New Jersey waterfront and one in Red Hook, Brooklyn -- faces strident local opposition and lawsuits.
In fact, while many recent headlines have focused on the battle between the city and states like Virginia, whose Republican Governor engaged in a public spitting match with Mr. Giuliani over garbage earlier this year, it appears that squabbles within the city and in New Jersey are much more likely to derail the city's garbage plans.
Outside the city, plenty of rural counties and municipalities with modern landfills or costly incinerators are eager to take the city's garbage -- and millions of dollars in fees.
"We have a virtual smorgasbord of disposal options," said Dennis C. Vacco, the former New York State Attorney General who became a vice president of Waste Management Inc., the biggest company seeking city contracts, shortly after he negotiated an antitrust settlement with Waste Management over a company merger in his previous job.
Kevin Farrell, the new city Sanitation Commissioner, is similarly confident. "Fresh Kills will close if I have to padlock it myself," he said.
 
The Power Broker
Borough President And Trash Czar
 Despite the looming concerns, there is at least one official in New York who is smiling: Guy V. Molinari, the longtime Staten Island Borough President.
Since the 1996 announcement, Mr. Molinari has become New York's unofficial trash czar, playing a central role in almost every city decision involving garbage. He confers with Mr. Lhota four or five times a week on the issue. He employs consultants to monitor city garbage policies and talks frequently about possible disposal sites with political leaders and landfill owners. He even handpicked Mr. Farrell, who as a former top Police Department official on Staten Island was a political supporter of Mr. Molinari.
Mr. Molinari, who in the late 1980's was one of the first city officials to promote Mr. Giuliani as a possible mayoral candidate, said the Mayor has returned loyalty with loyalty.
"You break your back and hope they won't forget it," Mr. Molinari said. "Giuliani hasn't turned me down in six years."
Mr. Molinari's success over the last three years has made him something of a hero to his constituents -- even though many are still skeptical that the dump will close.
"It's definitely a triumph," said Frank Reali, a real estate agent on Staten Island.
Mr. Reali said that when he attends real estate conferences around the country people invariably equate his home with garbage. "Anybody from anywhere else, they always say, 'You're from Staten Island? That's where that landfill is.' It'd be nice to have that stigma go away."
But Mr. Molinari's dominance of garbage policy has inflamed passions in other -- more Democratic -- parts of the city, whose trash burden will inevitably grow with the closing of Fresh Kills.
"This feels more like a vendetta to us than a plan," said Lou Somes, a leader of a Brooklyn group called Red Hook Against Garbage. Residents of the neighborhood, who complain that it is already flooded with trucks hauling trash from restaurants and offices, have bridled at the prospect of a giant new transfer station for household waste -- one of the proposals favored by the Sanitation Department.
Much of the criticism has focused on the task force the city set up in 1996 to forge a closure plan. The group, now disbanded, included representatives from a variety of Federal, state and city agencies; the offices of the Mayor and the Governor, and two environmental groups, but had local representatives from only one borough -- Staten Island -- and the representatives were Mr. Molinari and his daughter Susan, who was then a United States Representative.
"It's very bad for the city to divide itself like this," said Marilyn G. Gelber, who at the time was the city's Commissioner of Environmental Protection. "It's a way of doing government that's very limited, bars the windows, bars the doors, and you end up with a select group of people trying to develop public policies that affect the whole city."
Other critics say that by setting a firm deadline, Mr. Giuliani has lost leverage in negotiations with private haulers.
"The waste companies are dictating the agenda," said Barbara Warren, an environmentalist who was on the garbage task force but who now considers the resulting plan -- which has been revised several times by the Sanitation Department -- fatally flawed. "These huge companies stand to make immense amounts of money on disposal."
Over all, the Sanitation Department estimates that the cost of closing the landfill will more than double the city's garbage budget -- from $142.4 million in 1997, the last year when all trash went to the landfill, to $301 million in 2002, the first year that no trash will go there.
A former top Sanitation Department official said this situation was a result of the city's putting all its hopes in the private sector, with no alternatives.
But waste industry representatives say the city's best hope is the private sector, which -- in theory, at least -- is best positioned to find the most creative, efficient ways to move something no one wants to see or smell.
And at least the city is finally grappling with a realistic alternative to continuing to dump at Fresh Kills, said Chaz Miller, a lobbyist for the National Solid Waste Association, a trade group in Washington. "Fresh Kills is an environmental obscenity," he said. "People lose sight of that fact."
 
The Landfill
Staten Islanders Grow Weary of the Jokes
 Long ago, Fresh Kills was just the name of a rippling creek in a duck-filled marsh on the western shore of Staten Island.
But by 1938, Robert Moses, New York City's construction czar, began to see it differently. If the Sanitation Department filled it with garbage, he could build a highway between his envisioned Verrazano-Narrows Bridge and New Jersey. In 1948, a smelly hill started growing in the wetland.
Mr. Moses promised that the dump would close by 1951.
But that never happened, and ever since, Staten Islanders have been wary of promises. On a recent afternoon at the Staten Island Mall, shoppers expressed doubts that the landfill would ever shut.
"They've had like eight five-year plans to close it and they keep pushing it back," said Samantha Jacobs, 19, a Staten Islander who is studying advertising in Manhattan.
Mr. Molinari is determined to prove her and other doubters wrong. He sees his effort as a righteous battle to end Staten Island's status as the city's figurative and literal dumping ground. He notes that around the time Fresh Kills opened, six other dumps were operating in other boroughs. Over the years, they all closed, like thousands of similar leaky dumps nationwide, and every New York City incinerator was shut down, too.
Only Fresh Kills remained, growing so big that it is visible to orbiting astronauts.
The result has been an immense, smelly humiliation. Town houses now encroach on the landfill and its odors reach the exclusive Todt Hill neighborhood two and a half miles away. Mr. Molinari is convinced that nobody in the other boroughs cares.
"All other New Yorkers know is that their garbage goes someplace," he said. "We've been abused to hell and back."
By early 1995, Mr. Molinari had the opportunity he had been waiting for. Mr. Giuliani had been Mayor for a year and Mr. Pataki had just taken office.
His relationship to Mr. Giuliani was particularly close. In 1988, Mr. Molinari, then a Congressman, was the first politician to publicly trumpet Mr. Giuliani as a mayoral candidate, and it was Mr. Giuliani who persuaded Mr. Molinari to run for Borough President in 1989. "I know the guy like the back of my hand," Mr. Molinari said.
Still, he knew that he had to force the issue. As candidates, neither Mr. Giuliani nor Mr. Pataki had promised to close the dump, instead pledging lesser rewards, like lowering fares on the Verrazano-Narrows Bridge for Staten Islanders.
In May 1995, Mr. Molinari asked the borough counsel to prepare a Federal lawsuit against the city aimed at forcing it to close the dump, based on the contention that its emissions of methane and other gases violated the Clean Air Act. By February 1996, he was ready to sue, and he called Deputy Mayor Peter Powers. The Borough President said Mr. Powers had urged him not to sue, saying the Mayor would gladly issue an executive order to close the landfill.
But Mr. Molinari contended that the next Mayor might reverse course. "There's a lot of cynicism here on Staten Island," he said.
Undaunted, he filed the suit hours after the announcement of the impending landfill closure. For now, the suit is not being pursued, he said.
The courts were only one front in Mr. Molinari's battle plan. Another was the City Council, where Councilman Vito Fossella of Staten Island, now a Congressman, offered a bill to close the dump. More than half of the Council members supported it.
"It all happened very fast," said Mr. Powers, who is now a consultant to Waste Management, the largest of the garbage companies vying for city contracts. "Any one of these things would have made it happen."
In its pursuit of city contracts and smooth routes to its landfills in other states, Waste Management, which had $13 billion in revenues last year and is based in Houston, has retained a host of former political figures, including Thomas W. Corbett, a former Pennsylvania Attorney General, and Jody Powell, a Washington publicist who was press secretary to President Jimmy Carter.
Pressure to shut Fresh Kills was also building early in 1996, in Albany, where State Senator John J. Marchi, a Staten Island Republican, saw a chance to realize a 40-year dream. As a freshman Senator in 1958, he had drafted legislation to close Fresh Kills and other city landfills, only to see Gov. W. Averell Harriman veto the measure.
Now, Mr. Marchi, in an alliance with a Staten Island Democrat, Assemblyman Eric N. Vitaliano, was close to winning passage of a bill that would force the closure of Fresh Kills. They built support by adding a provision to kill the last incinerator proposal in the city, in the Brooklyn Navy Yard. This brought aboard Brooklyn Democrats.
Mr. Pataki's first reaction was that legislation was unnecessary, said Michael Finnegan, who was then his general counsel. The Governor could simply close the landfill on his own, something his advisers were urging.
But Mr. Marchi persisted. "I know you have every good intention, but people change," he recalled telling Mr. Pataki.
Mayor Giuliani took things a step further, urging the Legislature to move the deadline up by a year, to Dec. 31, 2001. The legislators did so, and the law passed overwhelmingly.
Larry Shapiro, senior attorney for the New York Public Interest Research Group, a supporter of closure legislation, said the law is ironclad. "A court could order garbage dumped in Central Park before it could order it dumped in Staten Island," he said.
Suddenly, city garbage policy had been completely, even dizzily, turned around.
In early 1996, Sanitation Commissioner John Doherty testified before the City Council about trash policies, and never even hinted that Fresh Kills might soon close. A sanitation planning document released that same year discussed keeping the dump going as long as possible. Even environmental experts eager to close Fresh Kills thought that it would take at least a decade.
"The wisdom of the time was there was probably another 10, 15 or 20 years before you really, really had to make this decision," said Ms. Gelber, the city's former environmental chief. "The view was that the Department of Sanitation could gradually get into this, learn how to do it, create the relationships. Then, when it was finally time to close it, you'd be in a good position to do so."
 
The Options
Simple in Theory, But Not in Practice
 Now, instead of facing a gradual transition, the Sanitation Department was thrown into a crash course on the politics and economics of exporting garbage.
Ways of handling the city's garbage have changed radically over the years, from scavenging pigs in the early 19th century to enormous rendering plants in remote areas after that.
These days, 860 collection trucks pick up residential garbage in the five boroughs and take it to one of seven marine transfer stations on waterfront piers. (An eighth station is temporarily shut down.) There it is dumped onto barges and taken to Fresh Kills, where cranes scoop the garbage into trucks that take it up the artificial mountain.
This system has enabled New York, unlike virtually every other big city, to take care of its own trash within city limits at a savings of hundreds of millions of dollars.
In theory, experts say, it should be easy to shift from the current dumping system to the new plan, which has two main thrusts. One is increasing the proportion of recycled residential garbage to 25 percent within four years, compared with 18 percent now.
But there is heated debate about whether this goal will be enough, a discussion Mayor Giuliani has animated by calling recycling a costly "frill" and its proponents "zealots." (After resisting for several years, he recently raised the recycling budget to pay for weekly pickups.)
In theory, sending the remaining garbage away should be simple. There are rail links on Staten Island and in the Bronx that could carry containers of garbage to any of the many landfills willing to take it.
But getting the trash from the other boroughs to these rail links is virtually impossible under the city's trash plan -- which grew out of the findings of the task force formed in 1996 -- largely because of provisions that were pushed by Mr. Molinari.
For example, the plan calls for "borough self-sufficiency," meaning that each part of the city must deal with its own garbage. This is fine for Staten Island and the Bronx. But it leaves Brooklyn, Queens and Manhattan with no easy answers, say representatives of those boroughs and independent waste experts.
The dominance of Mr. Molinari and his borough continues to anger other city officials, who are still struggling for solutions.
"This administration ignores everything everybody says and has secret meetings just with Staten Island," said Mr. Golden, the Brooklyn Borough President. "This is the most outrageous and heinous act I've seen in all my years in politics."
The Bronx Borough President, Fernando Ferrer, is more supportive of the self-sufficiency policy. "I am willing to take our own waste," he said. "We're willing to do our fair share."
To reassure constituents who doubted that Fresh Kills would ever close, Mr. Molinari also demanded that the city's new trash plan include steady reductions in the flow of garbage to Staten Island. And so far the city has been able to do that, using truck and rail shipments to carry garbage to private landfills, mainly in Virginia.
But as that flow grows and as the number of outbound trucks increases, roads, bridges and tunnels leading out of the city will be jammed, some officials and industry executives predict.
"There will be no escape from traffic nightmares," said Ronald A. Klempner, president of American Marine Rail of Englewood, N.J., which is bidding to build a transfer station for household garbage in the South Bronx.
In the meantime, city proposals for long-term solutions have been announced and have fallen apart.
In December, with some fanfare, Mayor Giuliani unveiled what was described as the permanent answer: a plan for city contracts with private companies to build three large transfer stations to process garbage for shipment to out-of-town landfills. One would be in Carteret, N.J., another in Newark and a third in the Red Hook section of Brooklyn.
But within hours of the announcement, red-faced city officials were forced to acknowledge that they had given New Jersey officials little advance notice of the plan, prompting Gov. Christine Todd Whitman to tell New York to "drop dead."
Deputy Mayor Lhota said in a recent interview that several other New Jersey cities have offered sites for transfer stations; he declined to name them. But other officials, in New Jersey and in the waste disposal industry, say the idea is dead.
Experts predict a similar fate for the Red Hook proposal. Under it, giant oceangoing barges would transport garbage down the coast and up Virginia's James River for disposal in landfills. That plan was initially held up by a lawsuit filed by Virginia and the shouting match between Mr. Giuliani and Gov. James S. Gilmore 3d of Virginia.
But the real problem is likely to be lawsuits from neighbors in Red Hook, who say they doubt that the high-technology plan promoted by Waste Management -- with sealed containers -- will be odor-free.
Similar suits have been filed by residents of Williamsburg and Greenpoint, Brooklyn neighborhoods that have seen growing nighttime caravans of city and private trucks hauling household garbage under the city's short-term deals with private haulers.
"The stench from some of those trucks is unbearable," said Annette LaMatto of Williamsburg, a member of several Brooklyn groups fighting the city's garbage plan. "It's like a million elephants."
Whichever solution finally emerges, it appears clear that Fresh Kills, like an aging dinosaur, is nearing the end of its era.
Most of the activity there now centers on the installation of pipes to capture leaking liquids and gases. State and Federal environmental officials recently said the cleanup was meeting standards. Only 150 acres of the 3,000-acre landfill remain an active dumping area. No equipment is being replaced, nobody is being trained and supervisors are preparing to retire.
Tugboats still pull net-covered barges in batches of four. Huge clamshell cranes dump the waste onto a concrete slab, where front-end loaders scoop it into trucks, which then rumble to an area marked with tall sticks representing the day's planned accumulation of trash.
The dumped trash is mashed by tractors, which make the spongy ground roll slightly, like a shaken Jell-O mold. And by nightfall, the giant hill has risen by 12 feet. But closure preparations move ahead. Eventually, the last active areas will be sealed and the landfill will become a grassy hill.
After leading a springtime tour of the dump, Stephen Violetta, 51, an operations supervisor who has worked there for 12 years of his 30-year Sanitation Department career, said he was retiring soon as well.
For his part, Mr. Molinari said he was confident that the dump would close. "We're going to have one hell of a big party," he said. "We'll have fireworks from one end of Staten Island to the other."
But he is prepared for the worst. The Federal lawsuit that he filed three years ago is still on the books and ready to be revived if anything goes awry.
If the dump does not close -- and close on time -- he promises to be a very angry man.
"We would go back to secession," he growled. "Or I would go up on the roof of Borough Hall and shoot myself."

Chart: "Running Out of Time"
Fresh Kills landfill is to close in 28 months, but plans for delivering the city's trash to other states remain unsettled.
 
Phasing Out the Old
For years, Fresh Kills served all of New York City. Trash was trucked to eight marine transfer stations throughout the city, loaded onto barges and floated to Fresh Kills. Staten Island trash was delivered by truck. Until recently, roughly 13,000 tons of residential trash was added each day. By Dec. 31, 2001, the city must divert all this rubbish outside the city.
 
Fighting Over the New
The simplest solution: use the same marine transfer stations, but take the trash to giant waterfront depots before sending it out of state. Some depots exist in Brooklyn and the Bronx, and other neighborhoods have been identified as possible sites for new depots. But community resistance, political missteps and a rule that forbids trash from one borough to pass through any other borough have rendered most of these sites unworkable.
 
Ambivalently Awaiting the Outcome
After the announcement of Fresh Kills' impending closure, states began bidding in earnest for contracts to import New York City garbage. Still, not everyone is salivating. In June, senators from various trash-importing states convened in Washington to push for Federal limits to interstate trash shipments. (pg. B4)

HEADLINE: One Last Mass, and a Group Moves On

BYLINE: By NINA SIEGAL 

 BODY:
The Rev. Luis Barrios began his final Mass at St. Mary's Episcopal Church in Harlem yesterday with a Spanish hymn accompanied by a Latin plena beat on tambourines.
"Sounds good already," said the Rev. Robert Castle, the rector, or the priest in charge, of the church. "I'm going to miss them." 
 The hymn was up-tempo, but the mood in the congregation was somber. Early this year, Father Barrios, an outspoken priest who has long had conflicts with the leadership of the Episcopal Diocese of New York over the church's policies and its mission, was told he would have to leave St. Mary's by Sept. 1.
His congregation has been meeting at the church at West 126th Street and Broadway since 1993, when Father Barrios was removed from his previous post at St. Ann's Church in the Mott Haven section of the Bronx. That removal came because of a conflict with the diocese that erupted over his blessing of same-sex marriages.
Father Castle, who has allowed political groups like the Black Panthers to meet in his church, then welcomed Father Barrios and asked him to offer services in Spanish. The Masses were such a success that Father Barrios soon had his own congregation, which the parishioners began calling the Iglesia Episcopal Santa Maria.
But with Father Castle, 70, scheduled to retire in December, St. Mary's vestry, or church council, has told Father Barrios that his congregation must find a new home so a new rector can begin with a clean slate. Father Castle's replacement has not yet been selected.
Yesterday, the 250-member congregation prepared for a nomadic existence by announcing that its next Mass would be held at the Cloisters.
Father Barrios urged his congregation to think of the change as a positive step, but said in private that he was disappointed with the vestry's decision, which he said was made without consulting members of the Spanish group.
"We got into a very rare and unique situation here where one part of the congregation was telling the other part of the congregation that they have to leave," he said. "I really believe the issue was ethnocentrism. They were not handling the fact that they have other ethnic groups within the congregation who were gaining power."
But Father Castle said Father Barrios's group at St. Mary's was a church within the church, not part of the larger congregation, which is English-speaking and 90 percent African-American. He said it was time for the Hispanic congregation to step out on its own. "We need to make a change and they need to make a change also," he said.
Some members of the Hispanic congregation say Father Barrios was asked to leave because of his political beliefs. When he was removed from St. Ann's, Father Barrios had also generated controversy through his support of programs like a needle exchange, a gay ministry and an outreach program to Puerto Rican political prisoners.
Father Barrios, however, said he does not bless same-sex union at St. Mary's, and Father Castle refused to comment on that issue.
"I think there's a concern that we're growing too big for the space and we're too political," said Rosita M. Romero, who is a leader of the committee trying to find the congregation a new home. She said the congregation supports independence for Puerto Rico and an end to economic sanctions against Cuba.
The Hispanic group has asked the Episcopal Diocese to help it explore options, one of which might be establishing a new church.
An official at the Episcopal Diocese said yesterday that the church's leaders were not available for comment. But Father Castle said the diocese had encouraged Father Barrios's congregation to apply to become a church.
Father Barrios said he would apply. "Each congregation is unique and is built on a kind of ministry that reflects a collective identity," he said. "I want to protect that."

HEADLINE: Shopping for Shoes, and Schools?

 BODY:

To the Editor:
While money may not be able to buy good teachers (Op-Ed, Aug. 23), it can keep poor and mediocre ones in the classroom. In New York City, economic incentives are not based on the quality of teaching but on longevity of service and accumulation of postgraduate credits. A teacher paid the maximum salary earns $70,000 a year, whether he has a Ph.D. in his subject area and is active in his vision for students or whether he is interested only in getting through the day while counting the years to retirement. 
 Good people will go into teaching if they perceive that it is a profession, not a job -- and that there is a career path with economic incentives based on knowledge of subject matter and the ability to transmit this knowledge to students.
 
ROBERT D. DIAMANT
Staten Island, Aug. 23, 1999
 
The writer retired as an assistant principal, New York City public schools.

HEADLINE: Einstein, Confused in Love and, Sometimes, Physics

BYLINE: By DENNIS OVERBYE 

 BODY:
Our story so far: A quiet mathematical prodigy grows up in a family of high technology schemers into a cocky, charismatic youth. He drops out of school and leaves his country to dodge military service. In college he alienates his teachers by cutting class. He breaks his mother's heart by falling in love with an older foreign woman and fathering an illegitimate child, and eventually marrying her.
Moonlighting at science, while working as a minor civil servant, he publishes a series of papers challenging conventional notions of space, time and the nature of light, and works his way up the academic ladder, becoming estranged from his wife, who is depressed and jealous of other women. He begins an affair in another city. 
 So has life episodically unfolded, over 12 years, seven volumes and thousands of pages of the Collected Papers of Albert Einstein, a massive effort to publish all the 43,000 or so documents Einstein left behind at Princeton's Institute for Advanced Study when he died in 1955.
This spring, the eighth and thickest installment of this saga, Volume Eight, was published by the Princeton University Press in two parts totaling 1,118 pages, carrying Einstein through four of the most heroic and stormy years of his life, from 1914 to 1918, when, among other things, he finished work on his crowning achievement, the general theory of relativity.
Its most startling revelation is that in the spring of 1918, Einstein considered breaking off his engagement to his cousin Elsa Einstein and marrying her 20-year-old daughter, Ilse, instead. And the new volume calls into question the degree of Einstein's commitment to antiwar politics in wartime Berlin.
Most importantly, though, the papers here illuminate the tortured and confused path by which Einstein finally completed his long quest to extend the principle of relativity to explain gravity and the universe. Even Einstein, it seems, found it hard to be Einstein.
"In these pages," said Dr. Robert Schulmann, a Boston University historian and director of the Einstein Papers Project, "we can closely observe Einstein on his solitary path to general relativity, on which personal relations are sometimes callously sacrificed in the name of scientific ambition."
Volume Eight marks middle age for Einstein and middle age as well for one of the most monumental and star-crossed efforts in the history of science. The collection has already transformed scholars' view of Einstein, shedding a sometimes unflattering light on his personal life, and launching a wave of new biographies and television specials.
"You just can't do any work, not just on Einstein but on early 20th-century science, without referring to these volumes," said Dr. Gerald Holton of Harvard University, a physicist, historian and Einstein scholar.
A warts-and-all approach was exactly what the founders of the Papers Project had in mind and the trustees of the Einstein estate feared. A publishing agreement between Princeton University Press and Hebrew University in Jerusalem, to which Einstein left his papers and which he helped found, was signed in 1971, but work was delayed for several years by a lawsuit filed by Dr. Otto Nathan, a Princeton economist and Einstein's executor.
Dr. Nathan, who was protective of Einstein's public image, challenged the appointment of Dr. John Stachel, a Boston University physics professor with whom he had clashed, as sole editor. Only a committee could do justice to the breadth of Einstein's interests, Dr. Nathan argued.
An arbitrator ruled in favor of Dr. Stachel in 1981, but the work was already off to a late start. It was further delayed by the spectacular discovery of Einstein's love letters to his college sweetheart and first wife, Mileva Maric, which disclosed the birth of their illegitimate daughter, Lieserl, in 1902. (She is believed to have been born in Serbia, and her fate is unknown.)
Meanwhile, Dr. Schulmann said, continuing research has only increased the project's work. Diligent scrounging in attics and archives, and the courting of collectors and descendants of Einstein's friends and family, have produced another 25,000 papers.
The original archive is now in Jerusalem and is open to scholars, as are copies in the libraries of Princeton and Boston University, where the Papers Project is headquartered. To the occasional chagrin of independent historians and journalists, the so-called supplemental archive that the project has assembled is not.
Moreover, there is another archive that is off-limits even to the Papers Project. An undetermined number of letters have been "sequestered," in Dr. Schulmann's phrase, in a classified archive at the Hebrew University until 2006. Dr. Schulmann said he did not know what was in the sealed archive and admitted it was "uncomfortable" for a historian to be in the position of presenting only a partial record.
The most significant discovery to date of the Papers Project has been Einstein's letters to Mileva.
Subsequent volumes have revealed unflattering details of Einstein's marriage, including the affair with Elsa and a bossy memorandum detailing the conditions under which he would go on living with Mileva.
The editors are unapologetic for airing dirty linen. "To me this makes him more human," said Dr. Schulmann, who said he still liked Einstein and admired the way he thumbed his nose at authority. "The Einstein we're trying to recover in these volumes is a living, breathing personality with faults. We want to see what kind of personal style he had and what kind of scientific style he had."
That style is vividly on display in Volume Eight, which Dr. Holton described as project's "high point."
"In many ways it was the best of times and the worst of times for Einstein," Dr. Holton said. He finished working out general relativity, but within a few months of his arrival, his wife and children had left him and World War I broke out, stranding him in a sea of German militarism and wartime deprivation.
Einstein's theory, which explains how matter and energy "curve" the geometry of space-time to create the phenomenon known as gravity, has been called one of the greatest intellectual feats of all time. Einstein completed the theory in the fall of 1915 after eight years of struggle. Intriguingly, Einstein had actually written down the correct formulation as early as 1912 or 1913, but then abandoned it in favor of a different version called the "Entwurf," meaning design or blueprint, which he had cooked up with his old friend, the mathematician Marcel Grossmann.
One of Einstein's strengths was that he trusted his gut feelings, said Dr. Michel Janssen, a professor of physics and philosophy at Boston University and co-editor of Volume Eight. But sometimes, Dr. Janssen said, he could fool himself.
For example, Einstein discarded his original formulation of general relativity by coming up with a problem he called the "hole argument," which addressed the question of whether the universe was uniquely determined by the arrangement of its contents. Einstein believed that it must be, and that his original formulation would violate that principle.
In the fall of 1915, however, when the Entwurf theory was falling apart for other reasons, and the mathematician David Hilbert of Goettingen University was also on the trail of general relativity, Einstein changed his tune. He adopted a resolution of the hole argument that he had previously rejected and reinstated his original theory.
"Einstein is good at believing what he wants to believe at various stages of the game," Dr. Janssen said. "In 1913, the last thing he wants is to escape from the hole argument. He has too much invested in his Entwurf. In 1915, he wants the hole argument to evaporate. Einstein was a bit of an opportunist."
Einstein's personal life was marked by a streak of callousness and aloofness, Dr. Schulmann said. Nowhere is that more evident than in the strange letter, dated May 25, 1918, that Ilse Einstein sent to her close friend Georg Nicolai, a doctor, well-known antiwar crusader and Einstein family friend, with the words, "Please destroy this letter immediately after reading it" scrawled across the top in big letters. At the time Einstein, who had moved to Berlin four years earlier to take a lucrative appointment to the Prussian Academy of Sciences and become director of the newly formed Kaiser Wilhelm Institute of Physics, was 38 years old and in the process of divorcing Mileva, presumably so that he could marry his cousin Elsa. Ilse, the older of Elsa's two daughters, was serving as Einstein's secretary.
The letter was a plea for advice. In it Ilse related how a simple "jest" one afternoon had suddenly escalated into a serious proposal that Einstein marry her instead of her mother. Einstein, she said, had confessed that he loved her. Moreover her mother, she reported, was prepared to step aside, if that was what would make Ilse happy.
"Albert himself is refusing to take any decision; he is prepared to marry either Mama or me," Ilse wrote. "I know that A. loves me very much, perhaps more than any other man ever will, he also told me so himself yesterday," she went on.
The feelings, however, were not quite reciprocal. Ilse loved Einstein like a father, she wrote, but she had no desire to be close to him physically. Her instinct, she confessed, was not to marry him. "It will seem peculiar to you that I, a silly little thing of a 20-year-old, should have to decide on such a serious matter; I can hardly believe it myself and feel very unhappy doing so as well. Help me!"
There is no evidence that the relationship with Ilse was ever consummated. Einstein and Elsa were married the next year and remained husband and wife until her death in 1936. (Ilse later married Rudolf Kayser, a writer and literary critic who subsequently wrote a biography of Einstein. She died of tuberculosis in 1933.)
Ilse's letter first came to the attention of the Einstein scholars in the early 1990's. At least one of the other editors, Dr. Schulmann said, thought the letter might be a product of Ilse's imagination. "I take it very seriously," said Dr. Schulmann, noting that Nicolai was a former lover of Ilse, as well as an older man, in whom it would be natural for her to confide.
He said he saw the letter and the situation it described as an example of Einstein's vaunted ability to detach himself from the people around him. "The most telling point is that he doesn't care," Dr. Schulmann said. "That's the Einstein we know. He is indifferent to the question of whether he marries one or the other."
Ilse and her confidant Nicolai also emerge in the new volume as key influences on Einstein's political activities during the war. Shortly after the outbreak of hostilities, Einstein was one of only three people to sign an "Appeal to Europeans" deploring the destruction of international culture, making him a hero to the pacifist movement, and he attended meetings of a pacifist political club, the Bund Neues Vaterland. Historians have long thought that Einstein drew up the manifesto, but the evidence, Dr. Schulmann said, suggests that it was actually written by Nicolai, an antiwar crusader, and author of a book called "The Biology of War."
But, again, detachment characterized Einstein's actions. At the same time that he was attending antiwar meetings, presumably at the behest of Ilse and Elsa, Dr. Schulmann said, Einstein consulted for a maker of submarine gyroscopes and even tried to design an airplane for the military. In his letters he often said that the war did not intrude on him. "Why shouldn't one let the servants live happily in the madhouse?" he wrote to a Swiss friend.
After the Bund was outlawed in 1916, Einstein stopped attending meetings and resisted Nicolai's continuing schemes. Einstein "identified with the underdog and the outcast," Dr. Schulmann observed, "but he was not prepared to march in the ranks."
The slow pace of publishing the papers has provoked criticism from other scholars. In an interview, Dr. David Cahan, a historian of German physics at the University of Nebraska, said that publishing a series of bound volumes over the years was too slow and costly and wrongly concentrated resources on the editorial process rather than on the historical process.
"I'd like to see the E.P.P. put everything on the Web and simultaneously transform its quarters at B.U. into a true and open research and conference center devoted to Einstein scholarship," he said.
In fact, Dr. Holton said, the option of publishing the Einstein papers in a facsimile form was considered back at the beginning and rejected in favor of the traditional style of extensive annotation and accompanying essays. Dr. Schulmann said, "The goal is to provide as thick a context as possible."
No one knows how long it will take the project to finish its task, which is projected to encompass at least 25 volumes. Dr. Schulmann said it would take another 10 volumes to carry Einstein's story to 1933, when he left Berlin and came to America. There is already so much material that project officials are beginning to leave some things out. Walter Lippincott, the director of the Princeton University Press, likes to joke that he will be dead and his children will be dead by the time the last volume rolls off the presses.
Dr. Holton is undaunted. He noted that it took a lifetime to publish the papers of Thomas Jefferson.

SECTION: Section E; Page 1; Column 2; The Arts/Cultural Desk 

LENGTH: 1662 words

HEADLINE: The Chills! The Thrills! The Profits!

BYLINE: By RICK LYMAN 

DATELINE: HOLLYWOOD, Aug. 30

 BODY:
In David Koepp's new film, "Stir of Echoes," opening on Sept. 10, Kevin Bacon plays a working-class lug from Chicago who begins to have unsettling supernatural visions after being hypnotized. On that same day moviegoers can watch Patricia Arquette experience strange apparitions of her own in Ruben Wainwright's "Stigmata."
Horror, it seems, is hot. First "The Blair Witch Project" came screaming out of cyber-nowhere to become the magazine-cover phenomenon of the summer. Then a little-heralded Bruce Willis creeper called "The Sixth Sense," which did not even highlight its star in its advertising, exploded at the box office and has refused to die, earning $138.9 million in its first 23 days of release and holding onto the No. 1 slot for four consecutive weekends. Has the mass film audience turned some sort of millennium-generated pop-cultural corner, sprouting a sudden and insatiable appetite for dark woods, ghostly visions and psychological horror? 
 Or is it just a really, really eerie coincidence?
"I feel that what happened is an immense coincidence," said M. Night Shyamalan, a 29-year-old writer, actor and director who sold the "Sixth Sense" screenplay to Disney a couple of years ago for $3 million, then among the highest prices ever paid for a screenplay.
"The surprise indie sleeper film of the summer happened to be a horror film," Mr. Shyamalan said, "and then my film came out right after it only because everyone shoved their movies toward the end of the summer to get away from 'Star Wars' and the new Austin Powers movie."
Coincidence may have been at work, but there is clearly some desire among filmgoers for a good, scary experience, one left untapped by "The Haunting," an earlier summer release that relied heavily on large-scale effects and was overwhelmed by the "Blair Witch" stampede.
"It never really goes away, this appetite for horror films," said Jeanine Basinger, chairwoman of film studies at Wesleyan University in Connecticut. "The millennium is approaching. We have all of these tragedies on our minds. In modern life it's just one damn thing after another, and we seek to explain it to one another. And if there's some experience that gives closure to it, gives an explanation or at least gives us reassurance that we're not the only ones having the scaries, it reassures us."
Robert Sklar, a New York University professor and author of "Movie-Made America," a highly regarded history of American filmmaking, agrees that if the spectacular success of these two recent horror films is more than a coincidence, it might find its roots in unease about the millennium. People, he said, are "spooked by all the things that are coming up at this time."
Mr. Sklar pointed out that the horror genre, one of the oldest in the movies, has always been cyclical, and that its moments of highest popularity have coincided with moments of extreme social and cultural dislocation.
Horror films like Robert Weine's "Cabinet of Dr. Caligari" in 1919 and F. W. Murnau's "Nosferatu" in 1922 were popular in Europe during the silent era, when the Continent was recovering from World War I. The golden age of Hollywood horror films came in the early 1930's, when Americans were struggling with economic depression. Directors like James Whale ("Frankenstein," "The Invisible Man," "Bride of Frankenstein") and Tod Browning ("Dracula," "Devil Doll") were establishing horror as a dominant genre.
In the 1950's, as Americans were coming to grips with the atomic age and the cold war, fears about annihilation and foreign aggression were played out in "The Day the Earth Stood Still" (1951), "The Invasion of the Body Snatchers" (1956) and numerous films about alien invaders, giant bugs and nuclear experiments gone awry.
The cycle, Mr. Sklar said, has always been the same: a burst of serious, creative films that eventually transform into comedies ("Frankenstein," for instance, becomes "Abbott and Costello Meet Frankenstein") before the right cultural moment engenders a revival. Perhaps, he said, the successes of "The Blair Witch Project" and "The Sixth Sense" show that the audience is poised for another such revival.
If so, it is a good thing for the filmmakers. The coming months promise a parade of either flat-out horror films or films with elements of supernatural or psychological unease, including David Fincher's "Fight Club," Martin Scorsese's "Bringing Out the Dead," Frank Darabont's "Green Mile," Tim Burton's "Sleepy Hollow," Peter Hyams's "End of Days" (in which Arnold Schwarzenegger does battle with Satan), Wes Craven's "Scream 3" and a remake of 1959's "House on Haunted Hill," directed by William Malone.
There is even a touch of old-style horror on the home-video front, with the release this month of two classics of the genre, including the original "Dracula," with Bela Lugosi, and George Romero's "Night of the Living Dead."
This sudden resurgence of the psychological horror film -- as opposed to its close cousin, the slasher flick -- has a lot of Hollywood executives asking themselves whether it is a fluke or a fundamental change in audience tastes. The betting seems to be that the back-to-back successes of "Blair Witch" and "Sixth Sense" was more of a quirky tremor than a tectonic shift.
"I think this has been more of a coincidence," said Dick Cook, chairman of the Walt Disney Motion Picture Group. "A few months ago no one had ever heard of 'Blair Witch.' Then all of a sudden it has become a huge, huge phenomenon."
Both Mr. Cook and Mr. Shyamalan acknowledge that they were worried that "Sixth Sense" would get lost in the hype surrounding "Blair Witch."
"That was the scary part," Mr. Cook said. "You certainly are concerned that with the momentum and the mountain of publicity and all that surrounded 'Blair Witch,' would 'Sixth Sense' attract an audience?"
Mr. Shyamalan said he was convinced that he had made a movie that could attract a large audience, perhaps even a huge one, and then "Blair Witch" came out of nowhere to draw all the attention.
"I couldn't believe it, man," he said. "I thought, 'Wait until the world sees we're going to be the big sleeper movie of the year.' There hadn't been a great horror film in years. Then 'Blair Witch' opens, people are going in droves, it's on the cover of Newsweek and Time. 'Oh man,' I thought, 'we're never going to get any box office.' My last two movies, nobody on the planet saw them. I thought, 'I can't believe it's happening again.' "
But "Blair Witch" didn't swallow up the entire horror audience. It seemed to have merely whetted its appetite. "The Sixth Sense" opened solidly in first place and has dropped only slightly over the subsequent weekends. There was also an interesting shift in the demographic makeup of the movie's audience over its four-week run, initially dominated by the 18-to-34-year-olds who are the traditional audience for horror movies and changing in its subsequent weekends to attract more younger and more older viewers, Mr. Cook said.
"There is no marketing you can do to create this kind of sustainability," he said. "Two or three movies a year have this kind of playability, and whenever you have that, you're sure to have some success. Then, once in a while, when all the stars are lined up perfectly, it turns into a cultural phenomenon which makes it into a mega-success."
Much of the success of "Sixth Sense" is attributed to strong word of mouth, since the film did not receive the kind of huge marketing blitz that often generates big summer hits. "People are coming out saying, 'Whoa!' " said Ms. Basinger of Wesleyan. "We don't see that much mature scare stuff. We've been in an era of slasher scare movies. These are schlock.
" 'Scream,' which I love, is winking at the viewer. This one does not wink at you and say, 'Hey, it's really O.K.' This is a movie that scares you down in the center, where it really hurts, a movie that speaks to real fear."
Wes Craven, who directed both "Scream" (1996) and "Scream 2" (1997) and whose "Scream 3" is due out in December, said that the success of "Sixth Sense" indicated not so much a rejection of gory horror as an appetite for all types of horror films, if well executed.
"I've never felt that gore is what makes something scary," he said. "It's all about whether you can intrigue an audience."
Mr. Shyamalan said he was particularly pleased that his film was a greater success this summer than "The Haunting," which in his view relied more on big special effects than on psychological tension and character development.
"Say it was reversed, and 'The Haunting' made $200 million, and we made $50 million," he said. "Studio executives would be saying, 'Well, that's what the audience wants, big special effects more than anything else.' So even if it's just temporary, at the moment people are saying, 'Hey, let's work on the character.' "
But several studio executives said they were reluctant to draw any hard conclusions about the back-to-back successes of "Blair Witch" and "Sixth Sense," which, they said, actually had very little in common outside of a few shared horror elements. Unlike "Blair Witch," "Sixth Sense" was largely uplifting and optimistic. "Any movie that is about death being not so bad or so final usually works," said Amy Pascal, president of Columbia Pictures.
Still, there is always a tendency to read too much into these pop-cultural coincidences, Ms. Basinger said.
"Two or three movies come out, and everybody wants to say: 'Is this a trend? Does it say anything about our society?' " she said. "You know what? Not a hell of a lot. These movies were developed two or three years ago in complete separation. Who knows what it means?"
Does she think moviegoers are turning away from gore and violence in favor of psychological horror and suspense? "You know, that'll be the day," Ms. Basinger said. "I don't think we've ceased to be bloodthirsty just because of Littleton, Colo."

HEADLINE: Bipartisan Apathy

 BODY:

To the Editor:
The political apathy among young people that Bob Herbert laments stems perhaps from the emerging centrist culture that is draining principle from the national discourse (column, Aug. 26). 
 America has long known a two-party system forged from polar ideologies. That system is now under attack by poll-sensitive politicians who have abandoned passion for political expedience. Though partisan squalor endures, Washington ideological rifts are increasingly shallow.
Consider the 2000 Presidential contest. It has been noted that the Democratic and Republican front-runners, Al Gore and George W. Bush, sound alike in their campaigns.
In centrist politics, all politicians sound alike. Passionate discussion of vibrant social issues yields to more bland discourse on the economy's health. It's no wonder that young people feel detached from the intricacies of Federal appropriations bills.
 
ANAND GIRIDHARADAS
Chevy Chase, Md., Aug. 29, 1999

HEADLINE: Home Buying Stays at Near-Record Pace

BYLINE: Reuters 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Aug. 30

 BODY:
United States consumers bought new homes at the second-highest pace on record last month despite rising mortgage rates, the Government said today.
The number of new single-family homes sold in July reached a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 980,000, the Commerce Department said. Although that was up only one-tenth of a percent from June, it was the highest sales pace since the record high of 985,000 in November 1998. 
 "These are very high levels of home sales," said Pierre Ellis, economist at Primark Decision Economics in New York. "It's a sign that people are willing and able to make big-ticket purchases."
But Mr. Ellis cautioned that a persistently high rate could add to the Federal Reserve's worries about inflation, possibly leading to another rise in short-term interest rates.
The Fed has raised short-term rates twice this year, once on June 30 and again last week. That has helped drive up mortgage rates, which are now about a full percentage point higher than they were in November, at the peak of new-home buying. But to the surprise of many economists, the climbing mortgage rates have failed to put much of a damper on housing demand recently.
"We keep doggedly insisting that some cooling down is coming, but it's clear the housing market still has some kick in it," said David Seiders, chief economist at the National Association of Home Builders.
In a further indication of strength in the latest report, the department revised the home-sales total for June to 979,000 from a previously reported 929,000. June sales were up 7.3 percent from May.
A strong job market, rising incomes and growing wealth resulting from the strength of the United States stock market are credited with fueling purchases of homes.
Mr. Seiders said his group's own surveys have been hinting at some moderation in the market, but he said many of the probable limitations would come from factors like shortages of wallboard and other building materials rather than any serious waning of consumer demand.
The housing figures are seen as portending economic strength, not just because the healthy demand is expected to encourage more home building, but also because the new homeowners will probably be shopping for items like furniture and appliances. That could increase retail sales and thus economic growth.
The Commerce Department said that the median price of a new home had edged up in July to $156,000 from $155,000 in June. But the average price was lower, at $186,000 compared with June's $190,700. The supply of new homes stayed steady in July at 3.8 months' worth -- a low level signaling robust demand.
The strength in home sales in July was led by the Northeast, where sales soared 33.9 percent, to 83,000. Sales rose 9.9 percent in the Midwest, to 178,000, and two-tenths of a percent, to 470,000, in the South. They fell 12.9 percent, to 249,000, in the West.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS;
Flow of Cash Into Stock Mutual Funds Rebounds in August

BYLINE: By Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
Net inflows into stock mutual funds rose an estimated 50 percent in August, rebounding from a slowdown in July, as stocks gained and interest rates declined.
This month, investors were on pace to buy $18.3 billion of shares in stock mutual funds, according to Trimtabs.com Investment Research in Santa Rosa, Calif. The inflows picked up steam in the middle of the month as benchmark stock indexes bounced off their lowest levels in five months. 
 In July, inflows fell to $12.3 billion from $19.2 billion a month earlier, according to figures released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute, the mutual fund industry's leading trade group.
"Over the past few years, you've had stretches of weak inflows, but it always comes back," said Russel Kinnel, mutual funds editor at Morningstar Inc., which tracks mutual funds.
At Fidelity Investments in Boston, a unit of the FMR Corporation and the biggest mutual fund company, net inflows to stock funds in August declined to $900 million through the end of last week, from more than $2 billion in July, said Anne Crowley, a Fidelity spokeswoman. The company's overall net inflows totaled about $4.3 billion, with $3.4 billion going into money market funds.
The Vanguard Group, the second-biggest fund company, said net inflows for August through last week slowed to $3.1 billion from $4.2 billion in July. Stock investments totaled $2 billion, down from $3 billion a month earlier.
The Charles Schwab Corporation, the nation's largest discount and on-line brokerage firm, reported net outflows of $159 million from stock funds through Friday. Funds focused on growth and aggressive growth companies lost a combined $411 million, said Morrison Shafroth, a company spokesman. Index funds posted net inflows of $188 million, Schwab said.
Over all, the biggest turnaround came in international stock funds, Trimtabs said. About $5.3 billion poured into foreign-stock funds, compared with net outflows of $700 million in July.
In the Trimtabs estimate, net buying of United States stock funds increased to $13.1 billion in August from $10.3 billion in July. Of the companies tracked by Trimtabs, 35 had net redemptions, 29 received new cash and 19 were little changed.
For the first seven months of the year, net flows into stock funds declined to $102.7 billion, the lowest since 1995, from $143.9 billion in the corresponding period a year earlier, according to the Investment Company Institute.

HEADLINE: The Russian Money Trail

 BODY:
The suspected Russian laundering of billions of dollars through the Bank of New York is giving the world a disquieting look into some of the darkest corners of Russian life. The financial threads that American and Russian investigators are now tugging on seem to lead most directly to the international operations of the Russia mafia. But even more important is the possibility that the inquiry will uncover evidence of corruption at top levels of the Russian Government, including illegal diversion of loans from the International Monetary Fund.
Because of the sensitivity of the investigation, and the political fallout it may produce as elections approach in both Moscow and Washington, it is critical that the inquiry be unflinching but fair. Politicians in both countries are already rushing to apportion blame for misconduct that has yet to be formally alleged, much less proved. There will be ample time to review the performance of Russian officials, and the Clinton Administration's handling of Russia, if high-level corruption in Moscow is confirmed. For now, the need is to investigate thoroughly and without fear of where the evidence may lead. 
 So far the only firmly established information is that at least $4.2 billion and possibly as much as $10 billion from Russia passed through the Bank of New York last fall and winter. Bank executives, eager to expand their overseas business, initially seemed happy to receive the deposits, even though the gusher of money should have raised suspicions. Investigators believe the banking transactions were designed in part to launder dollars collected by the Russian mafia, a varied array of criminal organizations that feast off the chaotic Russian economy. But the scale of the operation suggests that other sources of money were involved as well.
One theory, advanced by Mikhail Khodorkovsky, a Russian businessman under investigation in the inquiry, is that Russian officials used the bank to shield profits earned from the manipulation of Government securities before the ruble devaluation a year ago. Another line of speculation is that some of the billions of dollars in I.M.F. loans to Russia this decade were siphoned off by Russian politicians. In Moscow's hothouse atmosphere, these suggestions have added to charges, unsubstantiated so far, that President Boris Yeltsin, his family and his closest aides have secretly shipped millions of dollars abroad.
It is premature to judge any of these assertions. Certainly, conditions conducive to high-level corruption abound in Russia. Criminal syndicates are active, law enforcement is lax and in recent years a small group of Government leaders has managed the transfer of billions of dollars' worth of state assets to private hands. But the presumption of corruption in general, which is a reasonable belief, is far different from proving actual criminal conduct by specific individuals.
It is also a leap to suggest at this early stage in the investigation that the case shows the Clinton Administration has mishandled relations with Russia by supporting Mr. Yeltsin and his Government. The White House would have been irresponsible if it failed to assist the first democratically elected Government in Russian history. Whether the Administration was sufficiently sensitive to corruption problems in the Kremlin can and should be debated as the Presidential campaign here unfolds.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING;
BBDO New York enters cyberspace in a big way with an agency focused on interactive marketing.

BYLINE: By Patricia Winters Lauro 

 BODY:
BBDO NEW YORK, known for its television commercials for longtime clients like Pepsi-Cola, Visa and Frito-Lay, is making a big push into the exploding business of Internet marketing with the opening of a new agency dedicated to interactive marketing.
Called @tmosphere, the new shop is BBDO New York's vision of a hybrid agency that marries the technical ability of Internet marketing with traditional advertising techniques. To fulfill that vision, the firm is bringing in the top BBDO senior creative director, Mike Campbell, as its managing director. Mr. Campbell has worked on virtually all of BBDO's top accounts, creating memorable TV spots that have included the comical Donald and Ivana Trump spot for Pizza Hut and the much-publicized Mario Cuomo-Ann Richards spot for Doritos. 
 The new shop opens with capitalized billings of about $25 million from BBDO clients like Visa USA and General Electric and assignments for Federal Express, Frito-Lay, M&M/Mars and Pepsi One. That places @tmosphere squarely in the middle of the pack of the top 100 interactive ad agencies compiled by Advertising Age, the trade publication. In comparison, the nation's top interactive shop USWeb/CKS in Santa Clara, Calif., had $125.3 million in revenue last year, up from $54 million.
BBDO's move may foreshadow a coming consolidation in the interactive agency world as the Web has evolved from a hip, young medium into mainstream big business. Last year, $1.03 billion was spent on Internet advertising, up from $544 million in 1997, according to Intermedia Advertising Solutions, a unit of the Dutch conglomerate VNU Marketing Information Services.
BBDO's new agency is late to the field. Many of the big Madison Avenue agencies already have prominent interactive shops, like Ogilvy Interactive and Grey Interactive. "They're late to market," said Marissa Gluck, an analyst with Jupiter Communications in New York, an on-line research firm "I think they're going to have a tough time of it."
But BBDO said it believed its timing was actually opportune because the interactive business is now out of its infancy and ready for the skills of top-notch creatives. BBDO New York is part of BBDO Worldwide, which is ranked by Advertising Age as the world's third-largest ad agency.
"We've seen the engineering, the strategic planning and the placement, but we haven't seen breakthrough ways of interacting with consumers on the Internet -- no one is doing world-class creative," said Bill Katz, BBDO New York's president and chief operating officer.
Ted Sann, BBDO's co-chief executive and chief creative officer, said: "When you talk about building brands, that's not happening yet on the Internet. No one has cracked that code -- we're still in the very early stages of development."
BBDO has been involved in Internet advertising for about four years via BBDO Interactive, an in-house department that was mainly a media buying unit. The new agency will be involved in all aspects of Internet marketing -- from advertising to Web site development to strategy -- and will operate independently in its own offices apart from BBDO headquarters. The new agency, which will report to BBDO New York, will also seek its own new accounts.
BBDO's parent company, Omnicom Group, has also been intricately involved in the Internet and was one of the early investors in the fledgling interactive business via its Communicade unit, a portfolio of investments in interactive, E-commerce, digital design and technology companies. Omnicom has a stake in several independent interactive shops, including Organic, Razorfish, Red Sky and agency.com.
Indeed, @tmosphere will be headed by a Communicade senior consultant, Susan Pinkwater, who becomes the new agency's president and chief executive. Earlier, Ms. Pinkwater worked at agency.com, and even earlier, "in another life" she was a social worker.
In her work for Communicade, Ms. Pinkwater said she was delighted by the technical abilities of interactive shops, but was frustrated by the limited understanding of brand strategy.
"I want to create a new kind of agency that addresses branding, quality of creative and the big picture, and works with ad agencies instead of against them," said Ms. Pinkwater, who added that the staff of 25 was from both traditional advertising and Internet shops
But Ms. Gluck of Jupiter said advertising agencies had not made an easy transition to the Internet. BBDO's stellar creative reputation may have less meaning in the interactive ad world, where the hottest shops started as fledgling independents that have grown up with the Internet, she said.
'A lot of the traditional agencies are very stuck in their ways and not really open to the pace and flexibility that the Internet requires," Ms. Gluck said. "They haven't shifted their mindset."
Still, Ms. Gluck noted that it might be more economical to start up a shop and take talent from the field than to buy a shop outright because the valuations of many interactive shops "are astronomical." Several shops have gone public and there are rumors about more initial public offerings ahead, she said.
For Mr. Campbell -- who said joining @tmosphere felt a little like "being Joe Kennedy before the Court of St. James" -- @tmosphere has an edge because as interactive communications have evolved, they demand classic branding techniques. In their early experimental stages, interactive ads often had a completely different image than traditional (i.e. off-line) advertising and that diffused the message, he said. Interactive advertising -- like all advertising -- must go back to the core image of the brand, and that puts a big creative powerhouse like BBDO at an advantage, he said.
"The tools are different, the medium is different, but what's the same is how you bring a brand to life in a creative powerful way with a big idea," Mr. Campbell said.
Ms. Gluck likened the situation to the 1980's when the big shops gobbled up specialized medical agencies en masse with the growth of consumer health care marketing.
"I think there will be more consolidation among the larger agencies -- I think the traditional agencies are playing catch-up," Ms. Gluck said.
Mr. Katz agreed that consolidation was coming, but said it was premature to discuss. Still, he said, BBDO New York will succeed in the business.
"However it happens, it happens," he said.
