HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place;
Prosperity and Counting: A Time to Sell, Many Holders Say of Funds

BYLINE: By RICHARD A. OPPEL Jr. 

 BODY:
Lately, a lot of investors have found that one of the best things about mutual funds is selling them.
Don Matsanoff, 37, a commercial real estate broker in Columbus, Ohio, cashed out of a Dreyfus mutual fund in July and spent $24,000 of the proceeds on a sport utility vehicle. After enjoying strong returns for much of the 90's, he said, it was time to buy some things he had put off while he let his profits ride.
Most of his money remains in the market, he said, but he would rather take a profit on some of it "and use it for something, than have it sit there and watch it go up and down." 
 Another Columbus real estate broker, Wayne Harer, 47, recently sold about $150,000 in fund shares to pay for home remodeling. He could have obtained a home-equity or other loan, but with three children and college expenses looming, "I wanted to start whittling down that debt," he said.
"I just said, 'I'll take this amount off the table,' " Mr. Harer said.
Amid the market's record highs and volatile moves, many investors have decided to sell some of their funds and use the profit to remodel the bathroom, buy a new washing machine, take that expensive vacation or splurge in some way.
In fact, investors are cashing out stock mutual fund shares at the highest rate in almost a dozen years. Though more money is still flowing into funds than is being taken out, the pace at which people have been selling fund shares has been growing faster than the rate of purchases.
This selling helps to explain the surge in consumer spending on big-ticket items. Higher interest rates usually muzzle spending, but not this year. Home sales have soared, car sales are on pace to set a record, and retail sales have been very strong. By the method that the Federal Government measures things, consumers are spending more than they earn.
Investors typically sell 13 to 17 percent of their stock mutual fund shares in any given year. But that rate has grown substantially, and in July investors redeemed shares from fund companies at an annual rate of 21.8 percent, according to figures released yesterday by the Investment Company Institute, a trade group based in Washington. That is the highest level since December 1987, when investors were still pulling money out in the aftermath of the October crash.
Of course, the fund redemptions are not all being spent on consumer goods. Some proceeds are being reinvested into other funds, or perhaps into Internet stocks or other market darlings. But economists and fund-company executives say those cannot account for the strong pace of redemptions lately.
Many economists say that the giant profits earned in stock portfolios this decade have led individuals to spend far more than they otherwise would, thus helping keep the economy -- and corporate profits -- perking along. They break the "wealth effect" down like this: For every $100 increase in net worth, a consumer will spend at least an additional $3 or $4 a year.
With the recent increases in stock prices, that has translated into big spending gains. Total personal financial assets were $27.6 trillion as of March, nearly double the level at the end of 1992, according to the Federal Reserve. And that does not count gains in home prices. Borrowings against increased home values "influence consumer outlays beyond the effects of gains from financial assets," Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, said in a speech Friday, adding that run-ups in the prices of homes are at least partly related to increases in stock prices.
The growing importance of investment gains means that central bankers "no longer have the luxury to look primarily to the flow of goods and services, as conventionally estimated, when evaluating the macroeconomic environment," Mr. Greenspan said. In other words, central bankers must increasingly wrestle with the value of consumers' stock portfolios, homes and personal debts when setting interest rates.
Though economists agree that investment gains play a large role in consumer spending, they still differ on how significant the effect. A study last month by two officials at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, for example, concluded that stock market fluctuations affect current spending but "changes in wealth in this quarter do not portend significant changes in consumption one or more quarters later."
The decision by consumers to keep spending based on their increased asset values is a fragile one. The high rate of mutual-fund withdrawals suggests that consumers have been selling investments at a quick pace to maintain and upgrade their standard of living. Whether those sales continue, especially if the market swoons, could have big implications for the economy.
A. Michael Lipper, chairman of the fund research company Lipper Inc. in Summit, N.J., attributes some of the selling to the aging population. "A fair amount is due to people achieving their retirement" and resetting their financial goals, he said. "People are getting older."
But he also says that many others think the market may be overvalued. "They are reducing their equity commitment," Mr. Lipper said.
The high rate of fund redemptions unnerves John J. Brennan, chief executive of the Vanguard Group, who says he cannot figure why redemption rates would be higher now than during the market's nearly 20 percent selloff a year ago. "That's the mysterious issue now," he said. "People who hung in last year are paring back and taking some risk off the table."
Indeed, investor optimism about the stock market has dropped sharply, according to a Paine Webber Inc. poll of small investors. Optimism is close to the lowest level in three years, with 55 percent of the people surveyed optimistic and 18 percent pessimistic, according to the poll, which was conducted early this month by the Gallup Organization. The sample included 1,004 people with at least $10,000 each in assets to invest. When asked what they thought the market would do in the next three months, 28 percent predicted it would rise and 23 percent said it would fall.
To be sure, a lot more money is still flowing into stock mutual funds than out, buoyed by stable streams of contributions to 401 (k), individual retirement accounts and other such plans, which workers continue to feed each month to get tax breaks. After a rocky period following the market's drop a year ago, net fund sales are strong, if somewhat below the pace of the last two years. By this measure -- investor purchases minus redemptions -- investors poured $61.3 billion into stock funds in the second quarter and an additional $12.3 billion last month, according to the Investment Company Institute.
As the popularity of mutual fund "supermarkets" like that offered by Charles Schwab & Company has grown, some redemptions have gone into other mutual funds, though economists say they doubt this accounts for much of the rise in recent months. "Supermarkets have been around awhile" and can account for only a small part of the sudden increase in stock fund redemptions, said John Rea, the chief economist of the Investment Company Institute. Moreover, investor switching within the same fund families has stayed at about the same pace, as a percentage of total fund assets, for a half-dozen years, by the trade group's tally.
Some money from some redemptions is going into other investments, including individual stocks, as investors take advantage of low commissions available through on-line trading. In fact, the longstanding trend of investors selling shares of individual stocks slowed in the first quarter after having risen sharply for many years, according to data from the Federal Reserve.
Mr. Matsanoff, for example, the commercial real estate broker from Columbus, has transferred some money from funds to individual stocks in hopes of getting better returns. The fund he sold last month was performing well. "But my individual stocks were performing better," he said. "So when I decided which one I should pick in order to write a check for the truck, I thought my mutual fund was the way to go."
He is quick to say that he is not part of the on-line revolution, preferring the advice of his investment adviser, Donald L. Sazdanoff, of Sovereign Asset Management in Lexington, Ohio. "I see people getting killed" through Internet trading, he said. "I'd rather get the right advice from a professional than try to make a decision based on limited time for research."
Although consumer debt levels and personal bankruptcies remain high, many consumers have paid down debt recently or chosen to pay for new purchases with cash rather than credit -- a trend also attributed in part to the strong stock market. In a report this month, Moody's Investors Service said that in the second quarter consumers paid off the highest percentage of their credit card debt in a decade and that credit card delinquency rates fell in June to their lowest monthly level since April 1996.
But even as the rising stock market has helped propel the economy and strengthen the financial condition of the typical American household, "it's a two-edged sword," said David M. Jones, chief economist of Aubrey G. Lanston & Company, a New York bond dealer.
"If the stock market gets out of control, then there's a danger that spending will be excessive," Mr. Jones said. "And that will put undue pressure on the labor market and kick off cost-and-price pressures, forcing the Fed to tighten more than it might,"
"That could be a rude awakening," he added, "for the stock market, and consumers' balance sheets."

HEADLINE: Republic Services Warns of Weak Earnings

BYLINE: By Dow Jones 

 BODY:
Shares of Republic Services Inc. fell 32.6 percent in heavy trading yesterday after the company, a Florida-based waste collection concern, said that 1999 earnings would fall below analysts' estimates.
Republic closed down $5.375, to $11.125, on volume of about 16.9 million shares, which compared with average daily volume of about 800,000. Republic was the most active issue on the New York Stock Exchange yesterday. 
 Marc H. Sulam, an analyst for the Donaldson Lufkin & Jenrette Securities Corporation, noted that Republic's announcement came at an unfortunate time for the company and for the industry because of the uncertainty caused by Waste Management Inc., the industry leader. Since early July, Waste Management has changed management and lowered its profit outlook.
Late last week the much smaller Capital Environmental Resources said it was having trouble getting credit.
Now Republic Services, one of the most highly regarded companies in the industry, with H. Wayne Huizenga as its chairman, has released negative news. Early yesterday, the company said in a conference call to analysts that earnings for 1999, excluding charges, would be $1.17 to $1.19 a share, below the $1.25 that was expected by analysts surveyed by First Call/Thomson Financial. Republic reported pro forma profit of $1.01 in 1998.
Republic said that it was taking longer than expected to bring certain acquisitions, especially assets bought from Waste Management, up to its performance standards. Republic officials said that integrating the hauling operations purchased from Waste Management had presented the major challenge. The company, based in Fort Lauderdale, Fla., also said it was dealing with higher interest costs on its publicly issued debt.
On the call, executives said that the performance of Republic Services' base business was on target and that local economies in which the company operated were strong.
But analysts lowered their earnings views for the company for this year as well as for 2000.
Republic continues to seek large acquisitions but will scrutinize them more closely, the executives said. "The process is fine," said James E. O'Connor, the chief executive.
Republic said that its core business continued to perform as expected and that it expected internal growth of 6 percent to 8 percent for the year.
Mr. Sulam lowered his earnings estimates to $1.18 from $1.25 for 1999 and to a range of $1.35 to $1.40 from $1.50 for next year, but maintained his buy rating on the stock. Referring to the company conference call, he said management was able to help analysts "understand that the problems weren't at its core operations."
Michael Hoffman, an analyst for the Credit Suisse First Boston Corporation, lowered his 1999 earnings estimate for Republic to $1.18 a share from $1.25 and to $1.40 from $1.50 for 2000. He kept his buy rating on the stock but lowered his price target to $25 from $30. Deutsche Bank Alex. Brown cuts its rating to buy from strong buy.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS;
Shares Fall onRevived Fear Of an Interest Rate Increase

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
Stock prices fell for the third consecutive session yesterday, pulled down by worries that in early October the Federal Reserve may increase short-term interest rates for a third time this year.
A stronger-than-expected report on new-home sales for July and an earnings warning from a big personal automobile insurer combined to raise anxiety during morning trading. A selloff in the bond market, which pushed yields on long-term Treasury bonds to 6.05 percent, compounded the worries. 
 The result was a broad decline in equity prices, one that sent the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index to its biggest loss in nearly six weeks and battered financial service and transportation stocks. The S.& P. 500 fell 24.25 points, or 1.8 percent, to 1,324.02.
The Dow Jones industrial averagedropped 176.04 points, or 1.6 percent, to 10,914.13. Financial stocks like American Express and J. P. Morgan accounted for much of the drop.
Big losses in American International Group, the multinational insurance company, Bank of America and America Online generated the biggest loss in the S.& P. 500 index since July 20.
The technology-heavy Nasdaq composite shed 46.21 points, or 1.7 percent, to 2,712.69.
With many investors on vacation, trading volume was light: on the New York Stock Exchange, about 610 million shares changed hands.
Still, traders and analysts were not willing to say the lack of activity was a positive sign for the market.
"A lot of people will say that because volume is light this selloff doesn't mean anything," said William Meehan, chief market analyst at Cantor Fitzgerald. "I am not so sure. I told our clients this morning that this week is important."
Mr. Meehan and others are concerned that if reports later in the week -- particularly the August employment data, due on Friday -- portray an economy that is showing few signs of slowing down, the Federal Reserve Board will be forced to raise short-term interest rates again in early October.
The shift in sentiment, which the new-home sales numbers helped to promote, comes only days after most traders seemed convinced that last week's rate increase by the Fed was the last of the year.
"To me, this is just a replay of the cycle we played out in June, the last time the Fed bumped rates," said Michael Clarm, head of United States stock trading at Credit Suisse First Boston.
"We went into that increase with tremendous negative sentiment, and we did the same thing going in to last week. Now the anticipation is gone and we are still wondering if there is another hike down the road."
In addition to the threat posed by higher interest rates, insurance and financial service company stocks were hurt by an announcement late Friday from the Progressive Corporation, the nation's fourth-largest personal automobile insurer, that it would miss earnings estimates for the third quarter.
Progressive, an S.& P. component, fell 16 1/4, to 102 3/4.
The announcement dragged down other insurance company stocks.
Allstate, the largest publicly traded car insurer, fell 1 13/16, to 33 11/16. And the Safeco Corporation lost 1 9/16, to 34 5/8.
Diversified financial service company stocks also slumped. The Standard & Poor's financial stock index fell 5.34 points, or 3.9 percent, to 130.36. Citigroup fell 2 1/4 to 44 3/4. American Express dropped 4 15/16, to 139 11/16. General Electric, parent of GE Capital, fell 2 5/16, to 114 1/4. And J. P. Morgan lost 3, to 130 3/4.
By contrast, it was a pretty good day for drug stocks. Bristol-Myers Squibb gained 5/8, to 72 1/4. Eli Lilly rose 1 1/8, to 76 3/8. Pfizer rose 1/4, to 38 7/8.
Technology shares, including many Internet companies, fell after AT&T, following similar moves by MCI WorldCom and Sprint, announced the introduction of a discount long-distance rate. AT&T fell 1 1/2, to 46. MCI/Worldcom dropped 2 7/16, to 76 1/16.

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: BONDS;
Renewed Interest Rate Worries Send Treasury Prices Down

BYLINE: By ROBERT HURTADO 

 BODY:
Bond yields rose sharply yesterday on investor fears that the Federal Reserve would increase short-term rates again in the coming months as evidence of a strong economy continues to mount.
By late in the day, in trading volume that was lighter than normal, the price of the 30-year Treasury bond dropped 1 3/32, to 101, while its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, rose to 6.05 percent, from 5.97 percent on Friday. It was the highest level since Aug. 16, when the yield was 6.08 percent. 
 With investors growing cautious, the market's tone has shifted more toward selling rallies, traders said. Also weighing on the market was concern about the supply of new debt issues, as corporate treasurers scramble to sell securities before the end of the year. The reluctance among investors to buy bonds as the year wears on could lead to rising rates, as investors seek some reward for the higher risk to which they feel they might be exposed if Year 2000 problems occur.
"The massive supply of corporate debt has added to the liquidity squeeze," said Sung Won Sohn, senior vice president and chief economic officer at Wells Fargo in Minneapolis. "When corporations issue debt, the underwriters selling that debt reduce their exposure to losses by hedging those transactions."
Securities dealers of corporate transactions often sell or swap Treasuries ahead of these debt offerings, adding to the available supply of Government securities and thus placing upward pressure on rates. The securities dealers then buy back Treasuries when the corporate paper is in the hands of investors. The corporate supply calendar, however, is sparse this week, before an expected flood of issuance after the Labor Day weekend.
While the Fed has signaled that it may be finished raising rates for now, the door is not completely shut on further increases should the economy not show signs of slowing. Yesterday's report on new-home sales in July took an unexpected rise to its second-highest level on record, as sales rose one-tenth of 1 percent to an annualized rate of 980,000 units. Some analysts had expected 910,000. The increase trailed only November 1998's annual pace of 985,000.
Mr. Sohn of Wells Fargo, citing labor shortages, the weakening dollar, economic expansion abroad and a big rise in oil prices, said that the Fed "is not finished with tightening."
Sherry Cooper, chief economist at Nesbitt Burns Securities, thinks that bonds are vulnerable this week to the August National Association of Purchasing Management report, which is due out on Wednesday and which is expected to show a rebound after the outsized July decline, as well as to the closely watched employment and payroll report, scheduled for Friday.
The following are the results of yesterday's Treasury auction of new three- and six-month bills:

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Prosperity and Counting;
Stock Prices Aren't Forcing Fed's Hand

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

DATELINE: JACKSON HOLE, Wyo. Aug. 28

 BODY:
Like the narrator in a Greek drama, Yutaka Yamaguchi, deputy governor of Japan's central bank, took the lectern here and sadly described how the booming Japanese economy in the late 1980's came to tragedy. His description of Japan at its peak sounded eerily parallel to America in today's still evolving boom.
"A question haunts us to this day," Mr. Yamaguchi said. Would different policies have prevented the terrible crash of Japan's stock market and real estate prices and the decadelong recession that has followed? "With hindsight," he said, "such excellent performance was unsustainable." 
 Alan Greenspan, the chairman of the Federal Reserve, listened from the front row of long, narrow tables in the hotel conference room. Gene Sperling, President Clinton's chief assistant for economic policy, sat in the next row, just behind Mr. Greenspan. And most of the Fed's top policy makers -- the men and women with their hands on the crucial levers of the economy that control short-term interest rates -- were scattered through the crowded hall, with its invitation-only audience of prominent economists and high-level foreign officials.
The Federal Reserve's annual two-day gathering at Jackson Lake Lodge, across a vast green meadow and sparkling lake from the breathtaking Tetons range, has evolved from an informal get-together organized by the Federal Reserve Bank of Kansas City as a late August escape for central bankers into a high-powered forum for debating the issues that shape economies around the world. The big topic this year was the ever-rising American stock market. And almost as if they were staging a play, the Fed officials chose to act out, through research papers and speeches, how they were coping with its hard-to-control impact.
Mr. Yamaguchi played the heavy. Reviewing all that the Bank of Japan had done and failed to do, he left open the possibility that the Fed might also make mistakes, falling victim to roughly the same forces that brought down Japan in 1990.
"If there is a bubble in the American stock market," he said in an interview, "the outcome is difficult to predict when it collapses."
But if Mr. Yamaguchi's narration was foreboding, many of the Americans here said the last act of their own drama would end differently. The Japanese lesson has been well learned, they say. Further, the financial system in the United States, after its own wrenching changes early this decade, does not suffer from the debt problems that were Japan's real undoing after its stock market bubble burst. American real estate is nowhere near as inflated as Japanese property in the late 80's when, on the eve of the collapse, the land under Tokyo's Imperial Palace was supposedly worth more than all of California.
And if Wall Street crashes? Well, the Fed stands ready to minimize the damage, particularly with Mr. Greenspan still at the helm. After all, in the October 1987 market debacle, his skills in this department were aptly demonstrated.
"In all the discussion about crashes, we forget that the Greenspan Fed will react," said Mark Gertler, a New York University economist and the co-author, with Ben Bernanke of Princeton, of a new research paper that outlines how a central bank should proceed, particularly in the presence of very high stock prices. The Fed commissioned the study after learning that the research by Mr. Gertler and Mr. Bernanke reflected the thinking of Fed policy makers.
Mr. Greenspan carried his own suitcase when he came to these annual conferences in his early days as Fed chairman. Now, he moves about with an entourage of Secret Service agents. He has become a bigger-than-life figure in American public life, getting considerable credit for the nation's prosperity in recent years but likely to be the target of considerable blame if things go wrong.
So well known is his face that several of the tourists who shared the hotel with the conference delegates got as big a kick out of sighting Mr. Greenspan in the lobby as they did sighting moose from the hotel's huge back veranda.
"There is an increasing need to integrate into our macro models more complete descriptions of the responses of households and businesses to risk," Mr. Greenspan said in a speech on Friday, referring to the risks people take in buying stocks and real estate when prices are rising.
Stock prices in particular have become an increasingly significant subject of discussion at the Fed's periodic, closed-door policy meetings, where interest rates are set. "As time goes on," said Edward W. Kelley Jr., a Fed governor, "different parts of the economy have a greater impact than others. It was oil in the 70's, the very high dollar in the 80's, and there is little question that in today's world, the stock market is a very important component of the overall economy."
What role should the stock market play in shaping central bank policies? According to Mr. Bernanke and Mr. Gertler, the Fed quite correctly sets a flexible inflation target, an average to be achieved that now appears to be roughly 2 percent. Indeed, that is close to the actual annual rise in the Consumer Price Index these days. When inflation drifts above this level, or seems to be in the process of doing so, the Fed, in line with flexible inflation fighting, generally pushes up rates to try to slow the economy. And when economic conditions change in ways that suggest the inflation rate will fall below the target, rates are usually pushed down to stimulate borrowing and spending.
Always the output of goods and services is kept in mind: if the demand exceeds the potential supply, then rates go up, and if the opposite occurs, they go down.
Stock prices themselves, in this approach, should never be a target of interest rate policy. And in the Fed's deliberations, Mr. Bernanke and Mr. Gertler concluded, inflation has been the target, a point Mr. Greenspan also made in his speech here. Stock prices have been dealt with only as one component of inflation -- along with rising wages, shifting oil prices and the changing value of the dollaramong others.
Rising stock prices can feed through to inflation by making people feel wealthier, and companies, too. They spend or invest the new wealth or borrow against it and spend the borrowings. Demand rises and, if it exceeds the capability of the economy to deliver more goods and services, eventually the inflation rate should rise, too.
But are stock prices too high? They have risen, on average, 25 percent a year for five years, an unprecedented run. Or has the United States entered a new era of greater efficiency and profitability that justifies the higher prices?
Put another way, is the spending that emanates from rising stock prices excessive and inflationary? A number of the people here said so off the record, outside the conference room. Mr. Greenspan and his colleagues, however, insist that they do not know. And in that Hamlet-like failure to decide may lie the makings of a painful mistake: namely the Fed's failure to raise interest rates sufficiently to counteract the inflationary pressures coming from overvalued stocks, but not yet evident in the inflation numbers.
Japan fell into this mistake, Mr. Yamaguchi said. "The C.P.I. stayed close to zero for three years through 1988, while real growth accelerated to 5 percent per year from less than 4 percent in the early 1980's," he said. The impression grew that interest rates, in the absence of visible inflation, would stay low. And with Japanese interest rates low, the vision of healthy economic growth stretching endlessly into the future -- a vision popular in America today -- kept stock and real estate prices rising.
"The lag between rapid growth and the eventual rise in inflation happened to be longer," Mr. Yamaguchi said. "but the linkage didn't disappear."
When inflation did finally materialize, the Bank of Japan pushed up interest rates, giving the false impression, Mr. Yamaguchi said, that Japan had fallen into the most fatal flaw of all: pushing up rates to puncture the stock market bubble and not simply to fight inflation. "We have never targeted asset prices," Mr. Yamaguchi insisted.
Many outsiders disagree, arguing that Japanese officials left the clear impression at the time that they intended through higher interest rates to ease down the stock market because they feared the consequences of the market bubble.
Whatever the case, Mr. Bernanke and Mr. Gertler furnished a happier ending for America. Stick to inflation targeting, they say, and if there is a stock market bubble, let it burst. Acting much more quickly than the Japanese did, the Fed will reverse course, lowering interest rates and encouraging lending, thus minimizing the damage. The main potential damage is from falling prices (deflation) and from the unwillingness of frightened lenders to keep lending.
"If you think that over-reaction is a source of panic, confidence is restored if the private sector sees that the Fed will react," Mr. Gertler said, arguing that Mr. Greenspan's quick actions after the stock market crash in 1987 and the Russian default last year demonstrated his crisis skills.
"If people have confidence that the Fed will stabilize the economy," Mr. Gertler said, "that in itself will be a stabilizing force."
Equally important, American banks are far healthier than Japan's in 1990, without the mountain of bad debt, chiefly in real estate, that Japan's market crash revealed. That in itself will diminish the damage from a sharp decline in stocks, Mr. Gertler and Mr. Bernanke argued. And Mr. Yamaguchi agreed.
Still, a nagging worry remained. Is there more indebtedness and danger of default than the conference presenters acknowledged, perhaps among homeowners, hedge funds or other investors in the stock market?
Andrew Crockett, general manager of the Bank for International Settlements, raised that possibility. "Unpredictable things can happen," he said. "If there is a bubble and it breaks, people can turn out to have debts you did not realize were there."

HEADLINE: White House Seekers Wear Faith on Sleeve and Stump

BYLINE: By LAURIE GOODSTEIN 

 BODY:
Presidential candidates have always made a point of having themselves photographed as they step out of church on Sunday, carrying a Bible perhaps, and shaking the minister's hand.
But in the early campaign for the 2000 elections, the rite of political piety has moved far beyond the sacramental photo opportunity. The candidates are engaging in "God talk" that is more explicit, more intimate and more pervasive than at any time in recent decades, historians and political analysts say. 
 In a Houston church, Gov. George W. Bush of Texas describes his decision to "recommit my life to Jesus Christ." At a convention of black Baptists, Vice President Al Gore declares himself "a child of the Kingdom and a person of strong faith." And at a prayer breakfast in Philadelphia, Elizabeth Dole recounts a talk she had with God.
This is now common language on the campaign trail.
"In this Presidential contest there is much more discussion of personal faith than there has been in the past," said John C. Green, who studies how politics intersect religion as director of the Ray C. Bliss Institute of Applied Politics at the University of Akron, in Ohio.
"I suppose if you went far back enough in American history you'd probably find a year like this one," Mr. Green said. "But if you just think of the last 20 years, more candidates are talking more openly about their personal faith now."
The phenomenon is not confined to politics. This is an era in which public figures of all kinds are "coming out" about their private spirituality. Madonna gives interviews about studying cabala, or Jewish mysticism. The singer Lauryn Hill brings a Bible on stage to accept a Grammy award and reads a psalm aloud. The actors Richard Gere and Harrison Ford meditate at a Tibetan Buddhist ritual led by the Dalai Lama.
Politics, however, has its own rhythms, and there are particular reasons that candidates are now waving the flag of faith. The most urgent one, political analysts agree, is that in a nation weary of White House scandal, candidates must find ways to signal that they can be trusted behind the Oval Office doors.
"It's driven by the post-Monica hangover, almost like a drunk reaching for the black coffee," a Bush adviser said.
Thomas C. Reeves, a historian at the University of Wisconsin-Parkside, in Kenosha, and the author of "A Question of Character: A Life of John F. Kennedy" (The Free Press, 1991), said that the candidates "are trying to distance themselves from the squalor of the Clintons."
"The language itself isn't new at all," Mr. Reeves said. "James Garfield and Rutherford B. Hayes made the point that they were church members and spoke very staunchly about their faith, their church affiliations and the union of God and country. They did it because the politics of the gilded age were so squalid and so corrupt. Candidates, then and now, are trying to say that they stand above the mess that people perceive."
Both Mr. Bush, a Republican, and Mr. Gore, a Democrat, promote charities run by churches and religious organizations as an antidote to Government welfare. But this is not the first time that candidates have linked piety and public policy, Mr. Reeves said. Abolition of slavery, Prohibition, even Theodore Roosevelt's environmentalism were all proclaimed "godly" policies, he said.
But right now, the Presidential contenders are responding to polls showing that Americans are focused on immorality as the basis of many social ills. Asked whether they were more concerned about the nation's moral problems or economic problems, 58 percent of Americans said moral problems, while 38 percent said economic ones, according to a poll of 1,022 adults in a CNN/Gallup survey conducted in February.
"This has something to do with the Presidential scandals, and the school shootings like the one in Littleton," Mr. Green said. "There is a sense of disquiet in the land, and I think these candidates have picked this up in their polling, in talking to people. And when people are faced with existential issues, they tend to turn back to religion, and that's not just conservatives, it's everybody. Religion is a source of moral grounding."
Republican candidates in particular know they must find ways to appeal to the crucial evangelical voting bloc. For candidates like Gary L. Bauer, Steve Forbes, Patrick J. Buchanan and Alan Keyes, this is no challenge; they stand with religious conservatives firmly against abortion and homosexuality and for organized school prayer.
But for a candidate like Mr. Bush, playing to the center on those issues, his frequent religious testimony is "almost like a surrogate for the more hard-edged moral agenda of the party," said the adviser, who put Mr. Bush's position this way:
"What he is doing is like saying to the social conservative wing, 'I can't say everything you want me to say, or propose everything you want me to propose, but I want you to know that in my heart or in my soul, I'm where you are personally. And what I can accomplish, I will accomplish, and you can trust that.' In the end, I think that formula will work, that he will be able to get his fair market share of religious activists in the primaries."
Michael Cromartie, a senior fellow at the Ethics and Public Policy Center in Washington, said, "A little bit of religiosity can cover a multitude of sins, and it can cover a multitude of political sins.
"You can see it with Elizabeth Dole," Mr. Cromartie said. "A lot of religious conservative folk are really unimpressed with her ability to waffle on just about everything, but when they hear her talking religious language, they think, 'She may not be clear on abortion, and on a multitude of other issues I care about, but she loves Jesus.' "
Not all the God talk can be chalked up to mere posturing. Many of this crop of candidates are churchgoers who say they read the Bible regularly and look to God and Scripture for guidance. Mrs. Dole, long before she or her husband were Presidential candidates, gave speeches "witnessing" her turn toward God. After his Army service in Vietnam, Mr. Gore attended divinity school briefly. And Mr. Bush says his faith in God is what helped him give up alcohol.
When Mr. Gore summoned religion writers to the White House in May, he was asked whether he was trying to reclaim religion from the conservatives. "No," he said, "I'm just trying to be who I am."
One unspoken rule is that the only candidates who benefit by emphasizing their religious bona fides are Protestants. Michael S. Dukakis, of Greek Orthodox heritage and married to a Jew, avoided religious talk. John F. Kennedy, the only Roman Catholic President, had to deflect slurs about handing over the country to Vatican control. Mr. Buchanan and Mr. Keyes, both Catholics, use religious language that is indistinct from that of evangelical Protestants.
The evangelical talk of being "born again" that was greeted as exotic back in 1976 when used by Jimmy Carter is now commonplace. Jesse Jackson, an ordained minister and perennial Presidential candidate, helped make religious talk a campaign standard. And Mr. Clinton, a Southern Baptist, has displayed an ease with evangelical language and Biblical references that he put to use in his campaigns and as President.
It is paradoxical, says Nathan O. Hatch, professor of history and provost at the University of Notre Dame, that religious talk is so commonplace as the nation becomes increasingly religiously and ethnically diverse. "It's a far more diverse culture, so there's far less one can assume" about morality and religion, Mr. Hatch said.
Nevertheless, said Mr. Reeves, the historian, invoking "American civil religion, one nation under God, is almost a prerequisite" for a Presidential candidate today. "The public eats it up," he said.
"Wouldn't it be wonderful if you saw a candidate come along now who said, 'I'm an atheist,' just for a change of pace?" Mr. Reeves said. And what chance would that candidate have of winning?
"None," he said.

HEADLINE: Sports of The Times;
Light-Years And a Bridge Of Separation

BYLINE: By HARVEY ARATON

 BODY:
THE young ones are coming bigger, hitting harder, learning faster. From one end of the summer to another, London to New York, Mirjana Lucic schooled a former Harvard student on the existential differences between plucky challengers and aspiring champions, between opposite ends of the Whitestone Bridge.
Erika de Lone had pushed Lucic deep into a third Wimbledon set just two months ago in the first round of the tall Croatian teen-ager's run to her semifinal defeat by Steffi Graf. De Lone returned home to her best tennis summer ever, to become authentic queen of a gritty Bronx court. Then she walked onto Court 11 yesterday at the National Tennis Center, and it was all too clear that she was in the wrong borough, half a foot smaller than her opponent, in over her head at the United States Open. Against all reasonable laws of main-draw probability, Lucic was waiting for de Lone again, more poised, better prepared, still growing. 
 "It's amazing how much the tour has changed," de Lone said after Lucic had hammered out a 6-3, 6-3 victory. "When I first came on eight years ago, it was pretty much Seles and Capriati. Now you have so much depth, so many players, all these Europeans, so many big, strong players who are really cracking the ball."
It can be enough to send a woman who stands under 5 feet 7 inches tall and weighs 135 pounds to places where she can find someone her own size, where Darwin doesn't always dole out the prize money and computer ranking points. And this is how 26-year-old Erika de Lone of Lincoln, Mass., recently discovered another side of the tennis city, how she came to conquer the Bronx.
"It wasn't luxury like this, what you get used to," she said, making a sweeping gesture outside Court 11. "But the fans came out. The tennis was good."
Her pre-Open tuneup was the GHI Bronx Challenger, a satellite event in what is the tennis equivalent of the minor leagues. The site was Crotona Park, a short drive from the Third Avenue exit of the Cross Bronx Expressway. Here there were no fancy food courts, no parking lot full of luxury cars. The fans came in from the neighborhood on foot, for free. The kids came from all over town, courtesy of the New York Junior Tennis League. The players were bused up from Manhattan, on a yellow school bus that de Lone didn't have to ride because she'd brought her car.
She found a room at the Roosevelt Hotel at 45th and Madison for $133, a bargain compared to the $350 they wanted at the Parker-Meridian, which refused to extend her the Open player rate until the Open actually began. This is not a consideration for Venus Williams, but de Lone had to hustle everywhere she could to earn $53,137 in 1998. When Els Callens of the Netherlands retired from their Bronx Challenger final, it was worth $8,800 to her 1999 bank account, and a qualifier's place in the Open draw.
She had nine break points yesterday against Lucic and converted none. The match came and went, like the smell of hamburger wafting from close by. Still, it was the Open. It was another chance to step into a fantasy before it slips through her fingers, like sand at the beach. Her run from the Bronx was worth this one afternoon in Queens, against a 17-year-old who is supposed to be going all the places that Erika de Lone is not.
She dropped out of Harvard after her freshman year, with the support of her parents, who agreed that tennis was "now or never." Her older sister, Amy, played briefly on the tour. She has a younger half sister and half brother who are nationally ranked juniors. She's "taking this where it goes," though to reach her career-best ranking of 85, de Lone has had to win four Challenger events this year.
"I'm playing better than I ever have," she said, "but the players are so much stronger now, too."
The day she can't stand to wake up in a hotel room will be the day she starts thinking about returning to Harvard, about an economics degree. She realizes that day is coming, because more and more, the early round opponents are 6-foot sluggers, whether they are named Lucic or not.
While de Lone told her story outside Court 11, Amelie Mauresmo was powering forehands, impressively warming up for her first-round match. Venus Williams was about to bring her special athleticism into Arthur Ashe Stadium. Little sister Serena and Alexandra Stevenson will be out there belting winners tonight. It doesn't take a Harvard study to recognize what is happening in women's tennis, to know where the bridge to the future goes.

HEADLINE: As Students Return, Schools Cope With Severe Shortage of Teachers

BYLINE: By JACQUES STEINBERG

 BODY:
As children across the United States swarm back to school, principals from Washington to Palm Beach County, Fla., and from Houston to Los Angeles say they are struggling through the worst teacher shortage in recent memory. They describe being whipsawed by swelling enrollments and a push to reduce class sizes at a time when more teachers are retiring.
The problem is not one of overall supply: Nationwide, there are at least 180,000 recent graduates with teaching degrees and about 80,000 veterans looking for new jobs, enough to fill the estimated 200,000 teaching vacancies posted in the nation's public schools since last school year, experts said. 
The driving issues are geography and expertise.
Teachers in states where there are sometimes hundreds of applicants for one job, including Minnesota and Wisconsin, have been largely unwilling to move to those states most in need, including Texas and California. And even those teachers game for a change of scenery are often untrained in the subjects where shortages are greatest: mathematics, science, bilingual studies and special education, perhaps the most challenging assignment for teachers.
"It really is true that at this point, more districts are feeling the increase in demand," said Linda Darling-Hammond, a professor of education at Stanford University and the director of the National Commission on Teaching and America's Future, a private organization that has studied the issue. "In some cases they haven't planned ahead and done aggressive recruiting, and in others there really is a shortfall."
The impact has been felt in places large and small, from Texas, where there are an estimated 20,000 openings, to Belle Glade, Fla., a tiny city of migrant farm workers near Palm Beach, which was still searching for two first-grade teachers yesterday, two weeks after the school year began.
In pursuit of teachers, to say nothing of those trained in specific subjects, districts and states have stepped up the competition in ways that mirror the private sector and were unheard of even five years ago.
This summer, Washington began offering $1,000 signing bonuses to applicants certified to teach in the general classroom, $2,000 for those with a background in special education and $3,000 for anyone demonstrating proficiency in special education and an additional specialty. These incentives come on top of a $3,700 increase in starting salaries, to $31,000 this fall, and assorted other sweeteners like $500 for relocation assistance and $250 for school supplies.
In Texas, desperate districts persuaded the Legislature to approve a measure permitting them to rehire recent retirees, while allowing the retirees to keep drawing pensions.
And in the New Haven Unified School District in Union City, Calif., a district of 14,000 students outside of Oakland, the effort to attract out-of-town talent has become so sophisticated that candidates are directed to the nearest Kinko's copy shop to be linked live with district recruiters over video-conferencing lines.
"We just hired a special ed teacher from New York through the video interviewing," said Jim O'Laughlin, the personnel director for the district. "We've met her on TV, which is the same as sitting across the table."
In New York City -- which has tended to be among the last districts in the nation to complete its hiring each year, limiting its choices -- three early job fairs and a statewide recruiting push have paid a dividend. Officials of the city Board of Education said they believed they had hired enough teachers to reduce class sizes to 25, from about 28, in grades four through eight in its 40 lowest-performing schools.
But administrators said they had all but abandoned the goal of replacing hundreds of unlicensed math, science and special-education teachers in those schools and elsewhere because of a shortage of qualified candidates.
Across the country, numerous factors have conspired to place other districts in similar straits.
The nation's public-school population continues to bulge, with enrollment, which stood at about 50 million students in 1995, expected to reach 54.3 million by 2007, said Dr. Darling-Hammond of Stanford.
To meet that need, districts have to add 300,000 teachers, raising the number of teachers nationwide to 3.3 million by 2007, from 3 million in 1995, she said.
But that effort is already being hobbled by retirements because nearly one million teachers are between the ages of 45 and 53, with hundreds of thousands expected to retire when they reach 55, if not sooner. In New York City alone, union officials estimate that many of the 17,000 teachers who are eligible will retire after this school year.
And then there is the widening movement to reduce class sizes. In California, for example, a mandate to hold class sizes to 20 in kindergarten through third grade and in ninth-grade English and math has been expanded this year to include eighth-grade English.
That effort is having an effect on the quality of teachers hired, in part to meet such goals.
Kris Marubayashi, the associate director of the California Center for Teaching Careers, a state agency, said that about 1 out of 10 teachers hired there did not have the proper credentials. In many cases, she said, districts have been hiring candidates they rejected earlier.
"That's the sad story," Ms. Marubayashi said. "But you have to have someone in front of the class."
The Los Angeles Unified School District, which has hired 1,658 teachers for the school year that starts in two weeks, still has 2,500 vacancies, district officials said. Of those hired, 58 percent do not have teaching credentials, the district said.
Many teachers were hired based solely on their experience leading church or camping groups, said Antonio Garcia, the district's director of recruitment.
"We picked a lot of the field dry last year," Mr. Garcia said.
Indeed, in combing the nation's young work force for teachers, districts are often hopelessly outgunned in a thriving economy by technology and other companies that can sometimes triple a starting teacher's salary. School districts compete not only with each other, but also with private industry for the same dwindling pool of students who choose to major in science or math -- and who may choose a business close to home, rather than a classroom far away.
The teachers who specialize in the subjects that are in demand often would have to move thousands of miles to the nearest available job. That is because the surpluses of teachers tend to be clustered in the Midwest, where enrollment is growing more slowly, while the vacancies are elsewhere, often in states and inner-city districts that have experienced an influx of immigrants.
Further aggravating the crisis, students who commit to teaching while they are in college tend to choose elementary education, English or biology, or other subjects in which the demand is less acute. "A fairly high percentage of people who do graduate from teacher prep programs, it may be upwards of 40 percent, don't go into teaching," said Elizabeth Fideler, the executive vice president and chief operating officer of Recruiting New Teachers, a Massachusetts firm that works with states and districts.
Texas has been trying to turn back the clock to fill its vacancies -- an estimated 20,000 statewide, 300 of them in Houston alone.
Under an article imploring "adults 50 and older" to learn more about communicating via computer, the Aug. 26 edition of the Teacher Retirement System News beckons retirees of the Texas school system to "return to teaching on as much as a full-time basis."
Under a recently enacted state law, the retirees must be qualified in a shortage area, and have been out of the classroom for as long as 12 months, to keep their pension payments flowing. State officials had no immediate sense of how many retirees had taken districts up on the offer.
But such measures will not be necessary at Indiantown Middle School in Indiantown, Fla., near Okeechobee, which needed to hire 17 teachers this year. The school, hoping to reduce class sizes and to combat low test scores, succeeded in filling all jobs.
"Recruiting was very difficult this year because there is such a shortage out there," said Debra Harper, the school principal. "We had to go outside the county and even the state. Florida was a major draw for some teachers, and location helped us bring them down here."

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about teacher shortages in the United States referred imprecisely to the place where cash bonuses of up to $3,000 each are being offered to prospective teachers. It is Washington, D.C., not Washington State.
